Talaq — Divorce
beginner
6 min
General Rules: Who Can Pronounce a Valid Divorce?
Introduces basic capacity rules for valid talaq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid divorce (talaq) and identify which categories of people (minor, insane, sleeping, unconscious) render a divorce invalid.

Core Idea
A valid divorce requires a sane adult husband; coercion or intoxication does not invalidate, but minority, insanity, sleep, or unconsciousness does.

Divorce (talaq) is a serious legal act with specific capacity requirements. The Hanafi school holds that any sane adult male who utters a divorce, even if forced or drunk, has issued a valid divorce. However, those lacking legal capacity—minors, the insane, sleeping, or unconscious—cannot produce a valid pronouncement. This lesson establishes these baseline rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
The husband must be an adult (baligh) and sane (aqil). Only such a person can issue a valid divorce.
Adult means reached puberty; sane means having reason (aql).
2
Recognize the exceptions for coercion and intoxication
Even if coerced or drunk with a prohibited substance, the divorce is valid because the husband is mukallaf at the moment of utterance.
This is specific to Hanafi fiqh; Shafi'is hold intoxicated divorce invalid.
3
List those whose divorce is invalid
A minor (even near puberty and later ratification), an insane person (except conditional pronouncement made while sane), a sleeping person, and an unconscious person cannot divorce.
The minor's later pronouncement as an adult is a new divorce, not a ratification.
4
Understand the minor's ratification
If a minor divorces and later as an adult says 'I have pronounced it', it is a fresh pronouncement and valid. But saying 'I approve that divorce' does not effect it.
The pronoun 'that' refers to an invalid act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق
ويقع طلاق كل زوج إذا كان بالغاً عاقلاً. ولو مكرهاً، أو سكران بمحظور.
ولا يقع طلاق الصبي ولو مراهقاً، أو أجازه بعد البلوغ. أما لو قال: أوقعته: وقع؛ لأنه ابتداء إيقاع.
ولا طلاق المجنون، إلا إذا علق عاقلاً، ثم جن، فوجد الشرط.
ولا طلاق النائم؛ لعدم الاختيار. وكذا المغمى عليه.
ولو استيقظ وقال: أجزت ذلك الطلاق أو أوقعته: لا يقع؛ لأنه أعاد الضمير إلى غير معتبر. «جوهرة».
وقد علق الحموي في غمز العيون والبصائر على قول صاحب الأشباه والنظائر: «لا يقع طلاق المجنون إلا في مسائل: إذا علق...» مصوباً، فقال: «معنى قولهم: المجنون لا يقع طلاقه أي لا يصح إيقاعه، وحينئذ: لا صحة لاستثناء ما ذكره من المسائل، لأنه ليس فيها إيقاع».
أي: ومن المسائل التي يقع فيها طلاق المجنون: إذا كان عنيناً، وفرق القاضي بينه وبين زوجته بطلبها بعد تأجيله سنة بحضور وليه لعدم وصوله إليها، لأن المجنون لا يعدم الشهوة، فيكون هذا التفريق طلاقاً. ابن عابدين، الأشباه والنظائر لابن نجيم.
أي: إذا فرق القاضي بينهما في الحال بطلبها لجبه، فيكون التفريق طلاقاً. ابن عابدين، الأشباه والنظائر.
أي إذا أسلمت زوجة المجنون، وهو كافر، وأبى أبواه الإسلام: فإنه يفرق بينهما، وهو طلاق. الأشباه والنظائر.
Book of Divorce. The divorce of every husband takes effect if he is an adult, sane. Even if coerced, or drunk with a prohibited substance. The divorce of a minor does not take effect, even if he is approaching puberty, or if he approves it after puberty. But if he says: 'I have pronounced it,' it takes effect because it is the beginning of a pronouncement. And no divorce of the insane, except if he conditioned (it) while sane, then became insane, and the condition occurred. And no divorce of the sleeping person due to lack of choice. And likewise the unconscious person. And if he wakes up and says: 'I have approved that divorce' or 'I have pronounced it,' it does not take effect because he referred the pronoun to something not considered. From Jawhara. Al-Hamawi commented in Ghumz al-‘Uyun wa al-Basa’ir on the statement of the author of al-Ashbah wa al-Naza’ir: 'The divorce of the insane does not take effect except in cases: if he conditioned...' correcting him, and said: 'The meaning of their statement: the insane person’s divorce does not take effect means that his pronouncement is not valid, and then there is no validity to the exception of the cases he mentioned, because they do not involve a pronouncement.' That is, among the cases in which the divorce of the insane takes effect: if he is impotent, and the judge separated him and his wife at her request after giving him a year with his guardian present due to his inability to reach her, because the insane person does not lack desire, so this separation is a divorce. Ibn ‘Abidin, al-Ashbah wa al-Naza’ir by Ibn Nujaym. That is, if the judge separates them immediately at her request due to his [؟], so the separation is a divorce. Ibn ‘Abidin, al-Ashbah wa al-Naza’ir. That is, if the wife of the insane person becomes Muslim while he is a disbeliever, and his parents refuse Islam, then the judge separates them, and it is a divorce. al-Ashbah wa al-Naza’ir.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage from Al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Quduri discusses the basic requirements for a valid divorce (talaq) and then focuses on special cases involving the insane person. The matn (original text) states that divorce pronounced by a sane adult male takes effect, even if he was coerced or intoxicated with a prohibited substance. This is because he is a responsible legal agent (mukallaf) who intentionally uttered the divorce. However, the divorce of a minor (even one nearing puberty) is invalid, and if he later approves it after reaching adulthood, it is still invalid unless he makes a fresh pronouncement. Similarly, the divorce of an insane person is invalid, except in a specific scenario: if he conditionally made a divorce while sane, then later became insane, and the condition was fulfilled during his insanity, the divorce takes effect. For example, if he said 'If I do X, my wife is divorced' while sane, then became insane and did X, the divorce occurs. The sharh (commentary) then cites a debate from al-Hamawi in Ghumz al-‘Uyun: he objects to the exceptions listed in al-Ashbah wa al-Naza’ir for the insane person's divorce. He argues that the principle 'the insane person's divorce does not take effect' means that his own pronouncement of divorce is invalid, so the exceptions are not really about his pronouncement but about judicial separations. The commentator then lists three cases from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza’ir where the divorce of an insane person is considered effective: first, if he is impotent (inan) and a judge separates him from his wife after a year's delay at her request; second, if a judge separates them immediately for his impotence; third, if his wife converts to Islam while he remains a non-Muslim and his parents refuse to embrace Islam, the judge separates them, and this separation is considered a divorce. These are not pronouncing divorces themselves but judicial rulings that take effect as divorce. The passage thus clarifies that the ruling 'no divorce from an insane person' concerns his own utterance, not situations where the court effects a separation due to marital defects or faith disparity.

Key Terms
طلاق (talaq): Divorce, the dissolution of marriage by the husband's pronouncement.
صبي (sabi): A minor child who has not reached puberty; his divorce is invalid even if he later ratifies it.
نائم (na'im): Sleeping person; his divorce is invalid due to lack of choice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following divorces is valid?
A) A minor's divorce, later approved after puberty.
B) A sleeping person's divorce, confirmed upon waking.
C) An insane person's divorce, conditioned while sane.
D) A coerced man's divorce, but only if he was not physically forced.

Key Takeaway
A valid divorce requires a sane adult husband; minority, insanity, sleep, and unconsciousness invalidate the pronouncement, with the sole exception of a conditional divorce made while sane.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

The Insane Person's Divorce: Exceptions via Judicial Separation
Discusses exceptions to the rule that insane cannot divorce, focusing on judicial separations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the invalid direct divorce of an insane person and the valid judicial separations that are considered divorces, and analyze the critique of al-Hamawi regarding the exceptions.

Core Idea
An insane person's own divorce is invalid, but a judge's separation for impotence or faith disparity is deemed his divorce.

While the madhhab firmly holds that an insane person cannot pronounce a valid divorce, later Hanafi works list exceptional cases where a judge's ruling separates the couple and that separation is treated as a divorce from the insane husband. This sparked a debate among commentators like al-Hamawi, who argued that these exceptions confusingly treat judicial faskh as talaq. This lesson unpacks that nuance.

Guided Path
1
State the base rule
The insane person's direct pronouncement of divorce is invalid (لا يقع طلاق المجنون). This is the established ruling from Zahir al-Riwayah.
The only exception for direct pronouncement is a conditional talaq made while sane.
2
Recognize the cases from later works
Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir lists three cases where the divorce of the insane is said to 'take effect': (1) impotence with a one-year delay, (2) impotence with immediate separation, (3) faith disparity when wife becomes Muslim and husband's parents refuse Islam.
These involve judicial separation, not the husband's own utterance.
3
Understand al-Hamawi's objection
Al-Hamawi argues that the phrase 'divorce takes effect' (يقع طلاقه) means his own pronouncement (إيقاع) is valid. The exceptions do not involve his pronouncement, so calling them 'divorce' is incorrect.
This is a methodological critique about the scope of the term 'talaq'.
4
Appreciate the Hanafi response
Ibn ‘Abidin and others accept these as valid divorces because the judge acts as a proxy, and the practical effect is a divorce recognized for iddah and remarriage.
The ruling is accepted even if al-Hamawi's terminological concern is noted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ولا طلاق المجنون، إلا إذا علق عاقلاً، ثم جن، فوجد الشرط.

عنين (anīn): A man who is impotent, unable to have sexual intercourse.

Divorce of the Madman and the Sleeping Person: General Rule and Exceptions
Rulings on the validity of divorce by the madman and the sleeping person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general Hanafi ruling on divorce by a madman or sleeping person and identify the exceptional cases where the madman's separation is considered valid.

Core Idea
A madman's divorce is invalid, but the judge's separation in specific cases counts as divorce.

This lesson tackles a subtle area in Hanafi divorce law: the capacity to pronounce talaq. The general rule is that those lacking intellect or consciousness—the madman, the sleeping, and the unconscious—cannot effect a divorce. However, the Hanafi school recognises exceptional scenarios where a judicial separation involving a madman is treated as a divorce. We'll unpack the rule, the exceptions, and a critical refinement by al-Hamawi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and general ruling
The text first establishes that the divorce of a sleeping person and an unconscious person is invalid due to lack of choice. The same applies to the madman. Determine the fundamental condition required for valid talaq.
The condition is sanity and conscious choice (ikhtiyar).
2
Examine the madman's exceptions
The footnote explains that the madman's divorce occurs in three cases: (a) if the husband is impotent ('anayn) and the wife seeks separation after a one-year waiting period, (b) if the husband is castrated (majbub), and (c) if the wife converts to Islam while the husband remains disbelieving and his parents refuse Islam. Note that in each case the judge (qadi) effects the separation, not the madman himself.
These are not pronouncements by the madman but judicial decrees treated as talaq.
3
Analyze the sleeping person's post-waking claim
The passage states that if a sleeping person wakes up and says 'I intended that divorce' or 'I executed it', it does not take effect. Understand why: the intention must be simultaneous with the utterance, not retrospective.
Al-Jawhara is cited to confirm this.
4
Review al-Hamawi's critique
Al-Hamawi corrects the phrasing in Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir: the madman's divorce is fundamentally invalid, so the exceptions should not be called 'divorce' but rather 'separation' by judicial decree. Distinguish between the act of pronouncing talaq (which is invalid) and a judge's separation (which is valid and treated as talaq).
This is a semantic refinement, not a change in ruling.

The book of divorce. And the sleeping person. Or if he is impotent, or castrated, or his wife becomes Muslim while he is a disbeliever and his parents refuse Islam, as in Al-Ashbah. Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of divorce from persons lacking full mental capacity. The general rule is that the divorce of a madman (majnun) is not valid because he lacks sound intention. However, the Hanafi school recognizes exceptional cases where a judicial separation is considered a divorce: (1) if the madman is impotent ('anayn) and the wife seeks separation after a year's waiting, (2) if the wife converts to Islam while the madman remains a disbeliever and his parents refuse Islam. The sleeping person's divorce is invalid due to absence of conscious choice, and the same applies to the unconscious. If a sleeping person later claims he intended the divorce, it is not accepted because the intention was not present at the time. Al-Hamawi critiques the phrasing in Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir, stating that the madman's divorce is fundamentally invalid, so the exceptions should not be called 'divorce' but rather 'separation' by judicial decree.

Key Terms
مجنون (majnun): A person who has lost his intellect due to insanity; his pronouncements of divorce are generally invalid except in specific cases.
نائم (na'im): A sleeping person; his divorce does not take effect due to lack of conscious choice.
مغمى عليه (mughtama 'alayh): Unconscious person; same ruling as the sleeping person.
عنين ('anin): A man who is impotent and unable to consummate marriage; in Hanafi law, if the wife seeks separation and the judge grants it after a year's waiting, the separation is considered a divorce even if the husband is mad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Valid if he intended it
B) Invalid due to lack of choice
C) Valid only if he was aware
D) Valid but requires compensation

Key Takeaway
The capacity for valid talaq requires sanity and conscious choice; the madman's own pronouncement is invalid, but the judge may effect a dissolution that is treated as divorce in specific circumstances.

Divorce by a Slave and Explicit Divorce (Talaq al-Sarīh)
Rulings on slave's divorce and explicit divorce terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on the validity of a slave's divorce and identify explicit divorce terms and their effect.

Core Idea
A slave's divorce is valid when married with permission; explicit words like 'anti taliq' effect revocable divorce without intention.

This lesson covers two foundational rulings in Hanafi divorce law: who has the right to divorce in a slave's marriage and what words constitute an immediate, revocable divorce. Understanding these principles is essential for correctly applying the rules of talaq in various scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two distinct rulings
Read the passage and note that it addresses: (1) the validity of a slave's divorce, and (2) the definition and effect of explicit divorce words.
Both rulings are presented together in the text.
2
Analyze the slave's divorce ruling
Find the condition for the slave's divorce (marriage with master's permission) and the consequence (his divorce occurs, master's does not). Understand the underlying legal principle: the right of marriage belongs to the slave.
3
List the explicit divorce terms
Identify the three phrases given as sarih: 'anti taliq', 'mutallaqah', and 'qad tallaqtuki'. Recognize that they are used exclusively for divorce.
These terms do not require intention to take effect.
4
Connect the Quranic proof
The passage cites Quran 2:228 ('wa bu'ūluhunna aḥaqqu biraddihinna') to show that revocable divorce does not annul the marriage. Understand how the word 'ba'l' (husband) indicates the ongoing marital bond.
5
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Practice stating the ruling for a slave who says an explicit divorce term without intention, and confirm the revocable nature.

Commentary
This passage covers two distinct rulings. First, the divorce of a slave: If a slave marries with his master's permission, his divorce is valid because he holds the right of marriage (milk al-nikah). His master cannot divorce the slave's wife. Second, the definition and effect of explicit divorce (talaq sarih): The terms 'anti taliq', 'mutallaqah', and 'qad tallaqtuki' are considered sarih because they are exclusively used for divorce. When pronounced, they effect a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) without requiring intention, by virtue of their clear meaning and overwhelming usage. The Quranic verse (2:228) supports the revocable nature by referring to the husband as 'ba'l', implying the marriage tie persists. This is from Al-Hidayah commentary.

Key Terms
عبد ('abd): A male slave; in this context, one who marries with his master's permission.
صريح (sarih): Explicit words used solely for divorce, such as 'taliq', 'mutallaqah', 'tallaqtuki'. They effect divorce without need for intention.
كناية (kinayah): Implicit words that can be used for divorce or other meanings; they require intention to effect divorce.
طلاق رجعي (talaq raj'i): Revocable divorce where the husband can take back his wife during the waiting period without a new contract.

Divorce by a Slave with Permission
Ruling on slave's divorce and master's lack of authority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a slave's divorce and explain why the master cannot divorce his slave's wife.

Core Idea
A slave who marries with permission owns his marriage right; his divorce is valid, his master's is void.

This passage from Al-Quduri, expanded by the commentary, clarifies the authority over a slave's marriage. While the slave is owned, his marriage right is his own when contracted with permission. Therefore, only he can nullify it through divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two acts and their rulings
The passage addresses two acts: a slave divorcing his wife, and a master divorcing his slave's wife. Identify the ruling for each.
The slave's divorce occurs, the master's does not.
2
Locate the rationale
The commentary gives the 'illa: 'because the ownership of marriage is his right, so the nullification belongs to him.' Understand this as the reason.
The slave owns milk al-nikah.
3
Note the condition
The ruling applies only when the slave married with his master's permission. This is implied in the text.
Without permission, the ruling may differ (not covered here).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا تزوج العبد بإذن مولاه، ثم طلق : وَقَعَ طلاقه . ولا يقع طلاق مولاه على امرأته . (وإذا تزوج العبد بإذن ،مولاه، ثم طلَّق: وَقَعَ طلاقه)؛ لأن مِلْكَ النكاح حقه فيكون الإسقاط إليه. حقه، (ولا يقع طلاق مولاه على امرأته أي امرأة العبد ؛ لأنه لا حق له في نكاحه.
And if a slave marries with his master's permission, then divorces: his divorce occurs. And the divorce of his master does not fall upon his wife. (And if a slave marries with his master's permission, then divorces: his divorce occurs) because the ownership of marriage is his right, so the nullification belongs to him. (And the divorce of his master does not fall upon his wife, i.e., the slave's wife) because he has no right in his marriage.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn of Al-Quduri, expanded by the commentary. It addresses two situations: first, a slave who marries with his master's permission has full authority over the marriage contract, including the right to issue divorce; hence his pronouncement of divorce is valid and takes effect. Second, the master cannot divorce his slave's wife because the master has no ownership or authority over the slave's marriage. The rationale given in the commentary is that the slave owns the right of marriage (ملك النكاح) and thus only he can nullify it. This is a clear Hanafi position derived from the principle that the slave is the direct possessor of the marriage benefit even though he is owned.

Key Terms
العبد (al-'abd): The slave, a person owned by another, who lacks full legal capacity but can own certain rights with permission.
ملك النكاح (milk al-nikah): The ownership or authority over the marriage contract, which the slave retains with his master's permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Only the slave
C) Both master and slave
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The slave is the exclusive holder of his marriage right when married with permission, so only he can effect divorce.

Explicit Divorce in Hanafi Fiqh: Definition and Rulings
Definition, examples, and rulings on explicit divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define explicit divorce, identify its examples, state the ruling that it is revocable and does not require intention, and apply the rule that only one revocable divorce occurs even if a greater number is intended.

Core Idea
Explicit divorce words require no intention and always effect one revocable divorce.

This lesson unpacks a key ruling from Al-Hidayah on explicit divorce (al-talaq al-sarih). In Hanafi law, certain words like 'You are divorced' are so firmly tied to divorce that they are legally unambiguous. The passage explains why these words produce a revocable divorce without the need for intention, and why saying 'taliq' only counts as one divorce, even if the husband secretly intends three. We will trace the proof from the Quran and understand the linguistic reasoning behind the singular adjective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal category
The passage divides divorce into explicit (sarih) and implicit (kinayah). Focus on the explicit: words used exclusively for divorce. List the examples given: 'ant taliq', 'mutallaqah', and 'qad tallaqtuk'.
In Hanafi law, sarih words are legally certain markers.
2
Extract the ruling
The ruling is that explicit words effect a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i). This means the husband can take back his wife during the iddah without a new contract. Note that no intention (niyyah) is required.
Revocability is proven by Quran 2:228, where Allah calls the husband 'ba'l' during iddah.
3
Analyze the Quranic proof
The passage cites Surah al-Baqarah 2:228. The term 'ba'l' (husband) indicates that marriage is not completely dissolved; thus the divorce is revocable. Confirm that this is a qati' (definitive) dalil in both transmission and meaning.
The dalil is Quranic, so it cannot be overridden by istihsan.
4
Understand the limitation of number
The word 'taliq' is a singular adjective (na't fard). It cannot inherently express plurality. Therefore, if a husband says 'You are divorced' intending three divorces, only one revocable divorce occurs. The intention alone does not affect the number.
If the husband explicitly says 'three' or uses a number, it would count accordingly.
5
Apply the rule to a scenario
Imagine a husband says 'أنت طالق' (You are divorced) with the inner intention of making it three. According to the passage, the divorce counts as one revocable divorce. Mere intention without a verbal indicator has no legal effect.
This is a practical example of the rule that explicit words are interpreted solely by their plain meaning.

Explicit and implicit divorce: Divorce is of two types: explicit and implicit. Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Quran, explaining the category of explicit divorce (الطلاق الصريح). The author first defines explicit divorce as words that are used exclusively for divorce, not for any other purpose. The examples given are 'You are divorced' (أنت طالق), 'divorced' (مطلقة), and 'I have divorced you' (قد طلقتك). The key ruling is that such explicit words effect a revocable divorce (الطلاق الرجعي) without the need for intention, because their meaning is clear and unambiguous. The proof is the Quranic verse in Surah al-Baqarah (2:228) which refers to the husband as 'ba'l' (husband) during the waiting period, indicating that the marriage bond remains partially intact after a revocable divorce. The text then addresses a common point: if the husband intends more than one divorce (e.g., three), the divorce still counts as only one revocable divorce because the word 'taliq' is a singular adjective and cannot carry number. However, if he explicitly says 'three' or uses a number, it would count accordingly. The commentary further explains that the word 'taliq' is an attribute describing the woman, not a verbal noun, so it cannot accommodate multiplicity. The conclusion is that mere intention without a related verbal expression has no effect.

Key Terms
صريح (sarih): A word used exclusively for divorce, not for any other meaning, e.g., 'taliq'.
الطلاق الرجعي (al-talaq al-raj'i): A revocable divorce where the husband can take back his wife without a new contract during the waiting period.
نعت فرد (na't fard): A singular adjective, which cannot inherently denote plurality.
النية (al-niyyah): Intention; here, it is not required for explicit divorce because the words are unambiguous.

Ruling on Explicit Divorce Word 'Taliq'
States ruling on explicit divorce word 'taliq' as one revocable without intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when the explicit word 'taliq' is uttered and explain why intention does not affect the number of divorces.

Core Idea
The word 'taliq' is a singular description; it can only effect one revocable divorce regardless of intention.

In this lesson, we examine a key ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the explicit divorce word 'taliq'. The matn states that uttering 'anti taliq' results in one revocable divorce, even if the husband intends more. The commentary expands on the linguistic reasoning: because 'taliq' is a singular adjective, it cannot grammatically carry a number. Intention is irrelevant because the word is unambiguous. We will walk through the ruling, its proof, and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the husband uttering the explicit divorce word 'taliq' to his wife. According to the matn, the ruling is that one revocable divorce occurs.
The word 'taliq' is considered sarih (explicit) in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Understand the role of intention
The commentator states that intention does not affect the number of divorces. Even if the husband intends more than one, or an irrevocable divorce, only one revocable divorce occurs.
This is because intention is only needed to resolve ambiguity, and 'taliq' is unambiguous.
3
Analyze the linguistic proof
The proof is based on linguistic qiyas: 'taliq' is a singular description (na'tu fard). The dual is 'talqan' and the plural is 'tawaliq', so the word itself cannot indicate multiplicity.
If a number is intended, it is considered a description of a deleted source (like 'talaqan thalathan').
4
Identify the cross-madhhab difference
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) hold that if the husband intends three, three divorces occur. The Hanafi school insists on one due to the linguistic analysis.
The practical consequence is that in Hanafi the divorce is revocable, while in Shafi'i it could be three irrevocable divorces.
5
Apply the ruling
If a husband says 'anti taliq' three times in succession, the Hanafi ruling is still one revocable divorce, regardless of his intention.
Mere repetition does not change the ruling because the word is singular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يقع به إلا واحدة رجعية وإن نوى أكثر من ذلك، ولا يفتقر بهذه الألفاظ إلى النية .
* (ولا يقع به إلا واحدةً رجعية (وإن(1) نوى أكثر من ذلك) : أي أكثر من الواحدة الرجعية فيشمل الواحدة البائنة والأكثر من الواحدة؛ لأنه (٣) نَعْتُ فَرْد، حتى قيل للمثنى : طالقان، وللثلاث طوالق، فلا يحتمل العدد؛ لأنه ضده، والعدد الذي يقترن به نَعْتُ لمصدر محذوف، معناه: طلاقاً ثلاثاً (٤). «هداية»، ومجرد النية من غير لفظ دال : لا عبرة بها. (۳) (ولا يفتقر بهذه الألفاظ إلى النية ؛ لأن النية لتعيين المحتمل، وهذا مستعمل في خاص.
And it does not occur by it except one revocable [divorce], even if he intends more than that, and these words do not require intention. * (And it does not occur by it except one revocable [divorce] (even if he intends more than that): meaning more than the one revocable, so it includes the one irrevocable and more than one; because it (the word 'taliq') is a singular description, such that it is said for the dual: 'talqan', and for the triple: 'tawaliq', so it does not bear number because [number] is its opposite, and the number that accompanies it is a description of a deleted source, meaning: three divorces. 'Hidayah', and mere intention without a word that indicates it: no consideration is given to it. (3) (And these words do not require intention because intention is to specify the ambiguous, and this is used in a specific [sense].
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with expansion from the commentary. The matn states that when a husband says the explicit word 'anti taliq' (you are divorced), only one revocable divorce occurs, even if he intends more than one. The commentary explains that the word 'taliq' is a singular description (na'tu fard) derived from the triliteral root 'talaq'. As a description, it cannot grammatically carry a number; the dual is 'talqan' and the plural 'tawaliq'. If a husband intends more divorces, the number is considered a description of a deleted source word, e.g., 'talaqan thalathan' (three divorces), but the word itself only signifies one. The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah. The second part states that these explicit words do not require intention (niyyah) because intention is only needed to resolve ambiguity, and these words are unambiguously used for divorce.

Key Terms
طالق (tāliq): An explicit divorce word derived from the root 'talaq', used as a description of the woman.
رجعية (raj‘iyyah): Revocable divorce where the husband can take the wife back during her 'iddah without a new marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Three divorces occur, all irrevocable.
B) One revocable divorce occurs.
C) One irrevocable divorce occurs.
D) No divorce occurs because intention contradicts the word.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the explicit word 'taliq' is a singular adjective and always effects only one revocable divorce, regardless of the husband's intention or repetition.

Indirect Divorce Expressions (Kinayat) in Hanafi Fiqh
Definition and ruling on indirect divorce expressions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define kinayat and apply the rule that they require niyyah or dalalat al-hal to effect a valid divorce.

Core Idea
An ambiguous word needs a decisive context or intention to count as divorce.

We now examine the second category of talaq expressions: kinayat. These are words not originally coined for divorce but can imply it. Unlike explicit words, kinayat alone are insufficient; something must clarify the husband's intent. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category
Recognize that the text presents the second type of talaq expressions: indirect expressions (kinayat).
2
Define kinayat
From the text: words not coined for divorce but bear that meaning along with others.
Example: 'Ukhruji' (leave) can mean divorce or simply exit.
3
State the ruling
Divorce does not occur with kinayat unless there is intention (niyyah) or contextual indication (dalalat al-hal).
The text explicitly says 'لا يقع بها الطلاق إلا بنية، أو بدلالة حال'.
4
Explain the conditions
Niyyah: the husband's mental resolve to divorce. Dalalat al-hal: external circumstances like discussing divorce or anger.
Contextual indication can substitute for explicit intention.
5
Understand the rationale
Because kinayat are ambiguous (تحتمله وغيره), certainty required for divorce cannot rest on probability.
Quranic principle: divorce must be deliberate and clear.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والضرب الثاني : الكنايات وهي ما لم يوضع له، واحتمله وغيره، (ولا يقع بها الطلاق إلا بنية، أو بدلالة حال)، من مذاكرة الطلاق، أو وجود الغضب ؛ لأنها غير موضوعة للطلاق، بل تحتمله وغيره، فلا بد من التعيين أو دلالته ؛ لأن الطلاق لا يقع بالاحتمال. (1) ينظر الجوهرة النيرة ١٠٣/٢ .
And the second type: indirect expressions, which are [words] not coined for [divorce] but bear it and other [meanings], and divorce does not occur by them except with intention or contextual indication, such as discussing divorce or the presence of anger; because they are not coined for divorce, but bear it and other [meanings], so specification or its indication is necessary, because divorce does not occur by probability. (1) See al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah 2/103.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling statement about the second category of divorce expressions: indirect expressions (kinayat). The text defines kinayat as words that are not originally coined for divorce but can imply it along with other meanings. Examples include phrases like 'Ukhruji' (leave) or 'Ila hadhihi al-mun' (go to your family). The ruling is that such expressions do not effect divorce unless accompanied by the husband's intention to divorce, or there is a contextual indication (dalalat al-hal) such as discussing divorce or the husband being angry. The reason is that since the words are ambiguous, divorce cannot occur based on mere probability. This ruling is from the Hanafi school, relying on the principle that divorce requires certainty.

Key Terms
كنايات (kināyāt): Words that are not explicitly coined for divorce but can imply it along with other meanings, requiring intention or context.
دلالة حال (dalālat al-ḥāl): Contextual indication, such as the couple discussing divorce or the husband being angry, which clarifies that the ambiguous word was intended for divorce.

Key Takeaway

Kinayat: Implicit Talaq Expressions and Their Rulings
Defines kinayah and specifies three words that effect one revocable divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define kinayah, identify the three implicit expressions that effect one revocable divorce, and state the conditions under which they effect divorce.

Core Idea
Kinayah expressions require intention or context to effect divorce; three specific words yield one revocable divorce.

In this lesson, we examine the second type of talaq expressions: kinayah. Unlike sarih (explicit) words, kinayah are ambiguous and require the husband's intention or contextual evidence to become operative. The text specifies three words that, when intended for divorce, result in only one revocable divorce. We will analyze each word and the reasoning behind the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of kinayah
Kinayah are words not exclusively used for divorce; they carry multiple possible meanings, one of which is divorce. They are the second type of talaq expressions.
Compare with sarih (explicit) words that directly convey divorce.
2
Recognize the two prerequisites for divorce via kinayah
Divorce occurs only with intention (niyyah) or contextual indication (dalalat al-hal). Without either, the utterance remains ambiguous.
Intention can be inferred from context such as a quarrel or prior discussion of divorce.
3
List the three specific kinayah words
The three words are: 'I'tiddi' (observe the waiting period), 'Istabri'i rahimak' (declare your womb free), and 'Anti wahidah' (you are one).
Each word has alternative possible meanings unrelated to divorce.
4
Explain why only one revocable divorce occurs
The underlying implied phrase is 'you are divorced', which if explicit would yield one divorce. The implicit form is even more restricted, so only one revocable divorce occurs.
The Hanafi school restricts to one divorce; other schools may differ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والضرب الثاني : الكنايات، ولا يقعُ بها الطلاق إلا بنية، أو بدلالة حال .
(وهي) أي ألفاظ الكنايات ) على ضربين منها ثلاثة ألفاظ يقع بها الطلاق الرجعي إذا نوى الطلاق، (ولا يقع بها إلا طلقة واحدة، وهي قوله اعتدي ؛ لاحتمال أنه أراد: اعتدي نعم الله تعالى، أو نعمي عليك أو اعتدي من النكاح، فإذا نوى الاعتداد من النكاح: زال الإبهام، ووجب بها الطلاق اقتضاء، كأنه قال: طلقتك، أو أنت طالق، فاعتدي.
(و) كذا: (استبرئي رَحِمَك)، فإنه يستعمل بمعنى الاعتداد؛ لأنه تصريح بما هو المقصود بالعدة، فكان بمنزلته، ويحتمل الاستبراء ليطلقها حال فراغ رحمها : أي تعرفي رَحِمَك؛ لأطلقك. و: أنت واحدة؛ لاحتمال أنه أراد أنت واحدة عند قومك، أو منفردة عندي، ليس لي معك غيرك، أو نعتاً لمصدر محذوف : أي أنت طالق تطليقةً واحدة، فإذا نواه: جعل كأنه قاله.
قال في «الهداية»: ولما احتملت هذه الألفاظ الطلاق وغيره: تحتاج فيه إلى النية.
ولا يقع إلا واحدةً؛ لأن قوله : أنت طالق فيها مقتضى، أو: مضمر، ولو كان مظهراً لا يقع به إلا واحدة، فإذا كان مضمراً: أولى.
The second type: kinayat (implicit expressions). Divorce does not occur with them except with intention or by contextual indication. (They) i.e. the words of kinayat are of two types: among them are three words by which revocable divorce occurs if he intends divorce, and only one divorce occurs with them. They are his saying 'Observe the waiting period' (I‘tiddi) – because it is possible that he intended 'count the blessings of Allah' or 'my favors upon you' or 'observe the waiting period from marriage.' So if he intends observing the waiting period from marriage, the ambiguity is removed, and divorce is necessitated by implication, as if he said: 'I divorce you' or 'You are divorced, so observe the waiting period.' (And) similarly: 'Declare your womb free (istabri’i rahimak)' – because it is used in the sense of observing the waiting period; since it clearly states what is intended by the waiting period, it is equivalent to it. It could also mean 'know your womb' in order to divorce her when her womb is empty. And 'You are one (anti wahidah)' – because it could mean 'you are one among your people' or 'unique with me, I have no one else besides you,' or as an adjective for a deleted verbal noun: i.e., 'you are divorced with one divorce.' So if he intends that, it is as if he said it. Al-Hidayah said: Since these words admit the possibility of divorce and other meanings, they require intention. And only one divorce occurs because the phrase 'you are divorced' is either implied or understood. If it were explicit, only one divorce would occur; if it is implicit, it is even more so.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage defines kinayat (implicit talaq expressions) and gives their rulings. Kinayat are words that are not exclusively used for divorce; they could connote other meanings. Therefore, divorce only takes effect if the husband has the intention of divorce (niyyah) or there is a contextual indication (dalalat al-hal), such as discussing divorce or a state of anger. Among kinayat, three specific words effect only one revocable divorce when divorce is intended: 1) 'I‘tiddi' (observe the waiting period) – because it could mean counting blessings, but if intended for divorce, it implies the divorce itself. 2) 'Istabri’i rahimak' (declare your womb free) – because it directly refers to the waiting period after divorce. 3) 'Anti wahidah' (you are one) – because it could be an adjective for a single divorce. The commentary from al-Hidayah explains that since these words are ambiguous, intention is required to specify divorce. Moreover, only one divorce occurs because the underlying meaning is 'you are divorced' which, if explicitly stated, yields only one divorce; here it is even more restricted.

Key Terms
كناية (kinayah): An expression that is not exclusively used for divorce; it carries multiple possible meanings, one of which is divorce.
نية (niyyah): The mental intention of the husband to effect divorce when uttering a kinayah expression.
دلالة الحال (dalalat al-hal): Contextual evidence (e.g., a quarrel, discussion of divorce) that indicates the speaker intended divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Kinayah expressions require intention or contextual indication; three specific words (I'tiddi, istabri'i rahimak, anti wahidah) effect only one revocable divorce when intended.

Kinayah Words in Divorce: Classification and Effect
Classification of kinayah divorce words and their legal effects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of kinayah words in Hanafi divorce law and state the resulting divorce (raj‘i or ba’in) for each type when intention is present.

Core Idea
Kinayah words give divorce only when intended; three specific words give revocable divorce, all others give irrevocable.

In Hanafi law, not every word a husband utters that implies divorce carries the same effect. Some indirect expressions (kinayah) require a clear intention to divorce, and even then, the outcome differs: three specific words yield a revocable (raj‘i) divorce, while all others yield an irrevocable (ba’in) divorce. This lesson unpacks that classification and the legal reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of kinayah
Read the passage and note that kinayah words are divided into two types: three specific words (i‘taddi, istabri’i rahimaki, anti wahidah) and the rest. The three give revocable divorce when intended; the rest give irrevocable.
2
Understand why intention is required
Each of the three words has multiple possible meanings (e.g., i‘taddi could mean 'count blessings' or 'observe the waiting period'). Intention resolves the ambiguity. The passage cites al-Hidayah: 'since these words are probable, intention is needed.'
Hanafi usul: an ambiguous expression (mujmal) requires intention to specify.
3
See how divorce is established (iqtida’ and idmar)
For i‘taddi and istabri’i, the divorce is established by iqtida’ (necessary implication): the iddah logically requires a prior divorce. For anti wahidah, the word 'taliq' (divorced) is implicit (idmar) and understood from the context.
This reasoning shows the Hanafi reliance on linguistic inference.
4
Apply the rule: number and type of divorce
When intended, each of the three words gives exactly one revocable (raj‘i) divorce. The rest of kinayah words give one irrevocable (ba’in) divorce because they inherently imply finality.
The grammatical inflection of 'wahidah' does not affect the ruling, as laymen do not distinguish.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي على ضربين : منها ثلاثة ألفاظ يقعُ بها الطلاق الرجعي، ولا يقع بها إلا طلقة واحدة وهي : قوله : اعتدي، و : استبرئي رَحِمَك، و: أنت واحدة.
(وهي) أي ألفاظ الكنايات ) على ضربين منها ثلاثة ألفاظ يقع بها الطلاق الرجعي إذا نوى الطلاق، (ولا يقع بها إلا طلقة واحدة، وهي قوله اعتدي ؛ لاحتمال أنه أراد: اعتدي نعم الله تعالى، أو نعمي عليك أو اعتدي من النكاح، فإذا نوى الاعتداد من النكاح: زال الإبهام، ووجب بها الطلاق اقتضاء، كأنه قال: طلقتك، أو أنت طالق، فاعتدي.
(و) كذا: (استبرئي رَحِمَك)، فإنه يستعمل بمعنى الاعتداد؛ لأنه تصريح بما هو المقصود بالعدة، فكان بمنزلته، ويحتمل الاستبراء ليطلقها حال فراغ رحمها : أي تعرفي رَحِمَك؛ لأطلقك. و: أنت واحدة؛ لاحتمال أنه أراد أنت واحدة عند قومك، أو منفردة عندي، ليس لي معك غيرك، أو نعتاً لمصدر محذوف : أي أنت طالق تطليقةً واحدة، فإذا نواه: جعل كأنه قاله.
قال في «الهداية»: ولما احتملت هذه الألفاظ الطلاق وغيره: تحتاج فيه إلى النية.
ولا يقع إلا واحدةً؛ لأن قوله : أنت طالق فيها مقتضى، أو: مضمر، ولو كان مظهراً لا يقع به إلا واحدة، فإذا كان مضمراً: أولى.
وبقية الكنايات إذا نوى بها الطلاق : كانت واحدةً بائنة،
ثم قال : ولا معتبر بإعراب الواحدة عند عامة المشايخ، وهو الصحيح؛ لأن العوام لا يميزون بين وجوه الإعراب. اهـ
وقوله : فيها مقتضى، أو مضمر : يعني أن ثبوت الطلاق بهذه الألفاظ، إما بطريق الاقتضاء، كما في اعتدي، واستبرئي رحمك؛ لأن الطلاق ثبت شرعاً، لا لغة وإما بطريق الإضمار، كما في قوله: أنت واحدة؛ لأنه لما زال الإبهام بنية الطلاق: ثبت الطلاق لغةً على أنه مضمر فيه، بحذف الموصوف، وإقامة الصفة مقامه، وهذا شائع في كلامهم.
وقوله ولا معتبر بإعراب الواحدة... إلخ: احتراز عما قيل: إن رفع: «واحدة»: لا يقع به شيء؛ لأنه صفة للمرأة، وإن نصبها: وقعت واحدةً؛ لأنها صفة للمصدر، وإن سكن : اعتبرت نيته، كما في غاية البيان»، وتمامه فيها.
وبقية الكنايات) : أي ما سوى الألفاظ الثلاثة المذكورة، (إذا نوى بها الطلاق كانت طلقةً واحدةً (بائنةً)؛ لأنها ليست كناية عن مجرد الطلاق، بل عن الطلاق على وجه البينونة؛ لأنها عوامل في حقائقها، واشتراط النية : لتعيين أحد نوعي البينونة، دون الطلاق.
And they (kinayah words) are of two types: among them are three words by which revocable divorce occurs, and only one divorce occurs by them. They are: his saying 'i'taddi' (observe the waiting period), 'istabri'i rahimaki' (ascertain the purity of your womb), and 'anti wahidah' (you are one). (And they) meaning kinayah words are of two types: among them are three words by which revocable divorce occurs if he intends divorce, (and only one divorce occurs by them). They are: his saying 'i'taddi'; because it is probable that he meant: 'count the favors of Allah' or 'count my favors upon you' or 'observe the waiting period from marriage'. So if he intends the waiting period from marriage, the ambiguity is removed, and divorce becomes obligatory by implication (iqtida'), as if he said: 'I divorce you' or 'you are divorced, so observe the waiting period.' (And similarly) 'istabri'i rahimaki' (ascertain the purity of your womb), for it is used in the sense of 'i'tidad' (waiting period) because it is explicit about what is intended by the 'iddah, so it is of its status, and it is probable that he intends to divorce her when her womb is clear: meaning know your womb so that I may divorce you. And 'anti wahidah' (you are one) because it is probable that he meant: you are one among your people, or unique with me, I have no one else besides you, or an adjective for an omitted noun: meaning you are divorced once. So if he intends it, it is as if he said it. He said in al-Hidayah: Since these words are probable for divorce and other meanings, intention is needed in them. And only one (divorce) occurs because his saying 'you are divorced' in them is either by implication or by elision (idmar). If it were explicit, only one divorce would occur by it, so if it is implicit, it is even more so. And the rest of the kinayah words, if he intends divorce by them, they are a single irrevocable divorce. Then he said: And grammatical inflection of 'wahidah' is not considered according to the generality of the mashayikh, and that is the correct view because laymen do not distinguish between modes of inflection. End. And his saying 'in them is mqtada or mudmar' means that the establishment of divorce by these words is either by way of iqtida', as in 'i'taddi' and 'istabri'i rahimaki' because divorce is established legally (shar'an), not linguistically, or by way of idmar, as in his saying 'anti wahidah' because when the ambiguity is removed by the intention of divorce, divorce is established linguistically as being implicit in it, by omitting the described (mawsuf) and placing the adjective in its place, and this is common in their speech. And his saying 'And grammatical inflection of wahidah is not considered...' is a precaution against what was said: if 'wahidah' is raised (nominative), nothing occurs because it is an adjective for the woman; if it is in the accusative, one divorce occurs because it is an adjective for the source (masdar); if it is sukun, his intention is considered, as in Ghaayat al-Bayan, and the completion is in it. (And the rest of the kinayah) i.e. other than the three mentioned words, (if he intends divorce by them, they are a single irrevocable divorce because they are not kinayah for mere divorce, but for divorce on the basis of irrevocability (baynunah) because they are effectual in their realities, and the condition of intention is to specify one of the two types of baynunah, not the divorce.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining the division of kinayah (indirect expressions) for divorce in Hanafi law. Kinayah words are divided into two types: three specific words that, when intended, effect a single revocable (raj‘i) divorce, and all other kinayah words that effect a single irrevocable (ba’in) divorce when intended. The three words are: (1) 'i‘taddi' (observe the iddah), (2) 'istabri’i rahimaki' (ascertain the purity of your womb), and (3) 'anti wahidah' (you are one). For each, the author explains the linguistic possibilities and how intention resolves ambiguity. The proof relies on legal reasoning: since these words have multiple meanings, intention is required (as stated in al-Hidayah). The ruling that only one divorce occurs is because the implicit word 'taliq' (divorced) would only effect one if explicit, so a fortiori when implicit. The second part discusses the remaining kinayah words. They give a single ba’in divorce because they are not mere kinayah for divorce but for divorce with irrevocability. The author also clarifies that grammatical endings of 'wahidah' do not affect the ruling according to the majority of scholars, because laymen do not distinguish them. This is a fatwa position. The commentary concludes by noting that the condition of intention for other kinayah is to specify the type of baynunah (whether ba’in or raj‘i), not to establish divorce itself.

Key Terms
كناية (kināyah): An indirect expression that can mean divorce or other things, requiring intention to effect divorce.
الاقتضاء (al-iqtiḍā’): A legal construct where the law infers divorce from a statement that implies it, as in 'i‘taddi' where divorce is necessary for the waiting period to apply.
الإضمار (al-iḍmār): Elision of a word that is understood, such as the word 'tāliq' before 'wahidah'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Kinayah words for divorce require intention; three specific words yield one revocable divorce, all others yield one irrevocable divorce.

The Rule of Kinayah in Divorce: Intention and Default Number
Ruling on kinayah divorce: requirement of intention and default number.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kinayah divorce expressions, identify the requirement of intention, and apply the default number when intention is unspecified.

Core Idea
Kinayah words effect one irrevocable divorce only with intention; without intention, nothing occurs.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on indirect divorce expressions (kinayah). While explicit words like 'I divorce you' work without need for intention, kinayah—such as 'You are free' or 'Join your family'—require a clear intent to effect divorce. Moreover, the default is a single irrevocable divorce, even if the husband intended two. Understanding this principle is crucial to avoid unintended consequences in marital dissolution.

Guided Path
1
Define kinayah and distinguish from sarih
Kinayah is an indirect expression that requires intention to effect divorce, unlike explicit words like 'I divorce you' which work without intention.
Sarih (explicit) words do not need intention; kinayah always needs intention.
2
Identify the ruling with intention
If the husband intends divorce with a kinayah, it becomes one ba'in (irrevocable) divorce. Without intention, no divorce occurs.
The default number is one; see Lesson 2 for exceptions.
3
Explain irrelevance of grammatical case
The grammatical case of 'one' (wahidah) does not affect the ruling; the correct view is that it is irrelevant because common people do not distinguish.
Some earlier opinions considered case, but the relied-upon view rejects it.
4
Understand the mechanism: iqtida' and idmar
Some kinayah words effect divorce by iqtida' (requisition, as in 'observe your iddah') where divorce is legally implied; others by idmar (implication, as in 'You are one' where 'divorce' is understood).
This explains how the words legally cause divorce.
5
Apply to examples from the text
Use the provided list (e.g., 'You are free', 'Cover yourself') and state the haml with intention and default number.
The list is illustrative; the principle applies to any similar indirect expression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق

وبقية الكنايات إذا نوى بها الطلاق : كانت واحدةً بائنة، ثم قال : ولا معتبر بإعراب الواحدة عند عامة المشايخ، وهو الصحيح؛ لأن العوام لا يميزون بين وجوه الإعراب. اهـ

وقوله : فيها مقتضى، أو مضمر : يعني أن ثبوت الطلاق بهذه الألفاظ، إما بطريق الاقتضاء، كما في اعتدي، واستبرئي رحمك؛ لأن الطلاق ثبت شرعاً، لا لغة وإما بطريق الإضمار، كما في قوله: أنت واحدة؛ لأنه لما زال الإبهام بنية الطلاق: ثبت الطلاق لغةً على أنه مضمر فيه، بحذف الموصوف، وإقامة الصفة مقامه، وهذا شائع في كلامهم.

وقوله ولا معتبر بإعراب الواحدة... إلخ: احتراز عما قيل: إن رفع: «واحدة»: لا يقع به شيء؛ لأنه صفة للمرأة، وإن نصبها: وقعت واحدةً؛ لأنها صفة للمصدر، وإن سكن : اعتبرت نيته، كما في غاية البيان»، وتمامه فيها.

وبقية الكنايات : أي ما سوى الألفاظ الثلاثة المذكورة، إذا نوى بها الطلاق كانت طلقةً واحدةً بائنةً؛ لأنها ليست كناية عن مجرد الطلاق، بل عن الطلاق على وجه البينونة؛ لأنها عوامل في حقائقها، واشتراط النية : لتعيين أحد نوعي البينونة، دون الطلاق.

وإن نوى بها ثلاثاً : كانت ثلاثاً مغلظة، وإن نوى اثنتين : كانت واحدةً.

وهذا مثل قوله لامرأته : أنت بائن، أو بائنة، أو بَتَّة، أو بَتْلَة، أو حرام، أو حَبْلُكِ على غاربك، أو الحقي بأهلك، أو خَلِيَّة، أو بَرِيَّة، أو وهبتك لأهلك، أو سرَّحتُك، أو فارقتك، أو أنت حرَّة، أو تقنَّعي، أو تخمري، أو استتري، أو اغربي، أو اعربي، أو ابتغي الأزواج.

مغلظة،

وإن نوى بها طلاقاً ثلاثاً : كانت ثلاثاً؛ لأن البينونة نوعان: مغلظة وهي الثلاث، ومخففة وهي الواحدة، فأيهما نوى وقعت ؛ لاحتمال اللفظ.

وإن نوى اثنتين كانت طلقة واحدة؛ لأن الثنتين عدد محض، ولا دلالة للفظ عليه ؛ فيثبت أدنى البينونتين، وهي الواحدة.

وهذا مثل قوله لامرأته : أنت بائن، أو بائنة، أو بَتَّة، أو بَتْلَة، أو حرام، أو حَبْلُك على غاربك، أو الحقي بأهلك، أو خَلِيَّة، أو بَرِيَّة، أو: وهبتك لأهلك، أو سرَّحتُك، أو فارقتك، أو أنت حرَّة، أو تقنَّعي، أو تخمري، أو استتري، أو اغربي - بمعجمة، فمهملة: من الغُربة وهي البعد - أو اعربي - بمهملة، فمعجمة: من العزوبة، وهي عدم الزوج - أو اخرجي، أو اذهبي، أو قومي، أو ابتغي الأزواج، أو نحو ذلك.
Book of Divorce

And the remaining kinayah (indirect expressions) – if he intends by them divorce – are one irrevocable (ba'in) divorce. Then he said: And grammatical case of 'one' is not considered according to the generality of the mashayikh, and that is the correct view, because the common people do not distinguish between types of grammatical case. End.

And his saying: in it there is requirement or implication – meaning that the establishment of divorce by these words is either by way of requisition (iqtida') as in 'observe your 'iddah' and 'seek to clear your womb' because divorce is established legally, not linguistically; or by way of implication (idmar) as in his saying: 'You are one' – because when the ambiguity is removed by the intention of divorce, divorce is established linguistically as being implied in it, by omitting the described term and putting the adjective in its place, and this is common in their speech.

And his saying: 'And grammatical case of 'one' is not considered... etc.' is a caution against what was said: that if 'one' is in the nominative case, nothing occurs because it is an adjective of the woman; if it is in the accusative case, one (divorce) occurs because it is an adjective of the source; if it is in the genitive case, his intention is considered, as in Ghayat al-Bayan, and its completion is in it.

And 'the remaining kinayah' means besides the three mentioned words – if he intends divorce by them, it is one irrevocable divorce, because they are not kinayah for mere divorce, but for divorce in the manner of irrevocability (baynunah), because they are agents in their realities, and the condition of intention is to specify one of the two types of baynunah, not for the divorce itself.

And if he intends by them three, they are three severe (mghallaza), and if he intends two, they are one.

And this is like his saying to his wife: 'You are separated', or 'divorced', or 'cut off', or 'severed', or 'forbidden', or 'your rope is on your hump', or 'join your family', or 'a free woman', or 'a released one', or 'I grant you to your family', or 'I release you', or 'I separate from you', or 'you are free', or 'cover yourself', or 'put on your khimar', or 'veil yourself', or 'go away', or 'become a spinster', or 'seek husbands'.

Severe (mghallaza),

And if he intends by them a divorce of three, they are three; because baynunah is of two types: severe which is three, and light which is one, so whichever he intends occurs, because the wording bears it.

And if he intends two, it is one divorce; because two is a pure number, and there is no indication of the wording for it, so the lesser of the two baynunahs is established, which is one.

And this is like his saying to his wife: 'You are separated', or 'divorced', or 'cut off', or 'severed', or 'forbidden', or 'your rope is on your hump', or 'join your family', or 'free', or 'released', or 'I grant you to your family', or 'I release you', or 'I separate from you', or 'you are free', or 'cover yourself', or 'put on your khimar', or 'veil yourself', or 'go away' – with (first) a dotted (shin) then a plain (ha) – from 'ghurba' meaning distance – or 'become a spinster' – with a plain ('ayn) then a dotted (zayn) – from 'uzubah' meaning lack of a husband – or 'go out', or 'go', or 'stand up', or 'seek husbands', or similar to that.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling of kinayah (indirect expressions) in divorce. The author first states the ruling from the matn: for remaining kinayah expressions besides the three explicit ones, if the husband intends divorce, it becomes one ba'in (irrevocable) divorce. He then discusses two important points: (1) The grammatical case of the word 'one' (wahidah) is irrelevant for common people, as they do not distinguish between nominative, accusative, etc. The correct view is that it is not considered, contrary to some who said its case determines occurrence. (2) The mechanism of divorce with these words: either by iqtida' (requisition) as in 'observe your 'iddah' where the divorce is legally understood, or by idmar (implication) as in 'You are one' where the word 'divorce' is omitted and understood from context. The author then explains the purpose of requiring intention: it is not to establish the divorce itself but to specify which type of baynunah (separation) is intended – either the light (one talaq) or the severe (three talaq). This is because these kinayah words are not mere metaphors for divorce but inherently connote separation. They are agents (عوامل) in their realities, so they effect divorce with intention. The passage then addresses the scenario of intending a specific number: if the husband intends three divorces, three occur (severe), and if he intends two, only one occurs because the wording lacks an indication for the specific number two, so the lesser (one) is established. This is a key principle in Hanafi fiqh: when a word can carry multiple meanings and the intention is for an intermediate number not linguistically supported, the minimum applies. The passage concludes with an extensive list of example kinayah phrases from classical texts, some with explanatory glosses on their etymology (e.g., 'aghribi' from ghurba meaning distance, 'u'rubi' from 'uzubah meaning spinsterhood).

Key Terms
كناية (kinayah): An indirect expression that does not explicitly mention divorce but can imply it by context.
بائن (ba'in): Irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately without right of return except by new contract.
مغلظة (mghallaza): Severe divorce that occurs with three pronouncements, requiring a halalah (marriage to another man) to remarry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is true about kinayah divorce according to the Hanafi school?
A) It effects divorce without intention.
B) It requires intention and defaults to one ba'in divorce.
C) It always effects three divorces.
D) It is not recognized as valid divorce.

Key Takeaway
Kinayah expressions in divorce require the husband's intention, and without specification, only one irrevocable divorce occurs.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

Intending a Specific Number of Divorces with Kinayah
Ruling on intending a specific number of divorces with kinayah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rule when a husband uses kinayah and intends a specific number of divorces, and apply the rule that intending two results in one.

Core Idea
When a husband intends three divorces by kinayah, three occur; if he intends two, only one, because the word lacks indication for two.

Sometimes a husband may intend a specific number of divorces when using indirect expressions. Hanafi law carefully distinguishes between the numbers. While intending three is linguistically plausible (both 'light' and 'severe' separations), intending two finds no support in the word's meaning, so the lesser (one) is applied. This lesson examines that complex rule and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two types of baynunah
Baynunah (separation) is of two types: light (one talaq) and severe (three talaq). Kinayah words indicate this general concept.
The word 'baynunah' itself does not specify number.
2
Intention of three is effective
If the husband intends three divorces, three occur because the wording can bear the severe type of baynunah.
This is because three is one of the two possible types.
3
Intention of two is ineffective
If the husband intends two divorces, only one occurs. Two is a 'pure number' with no linguistic indication in the kinayah expressions.
The reasoning: the word only indicates one or three, not an arbitrary number.
4
Apply the rule to examples
Using the list, test scenarios: if he says 'ba'in' intending three, three occur; if intending two, one occurs.
The same applies to all kinayah expressions in the text.
5
Recognize the principle
When a word has a range of meanings and the intended number is not supported by the word, the minimum applies.
This is a usul al-fiqh principle applied here to divorce.

كتاب الطلاق

وبقية الكنايات إذا نوى بها الطلاق : كانت واحدةً بائنة، ثم قال : ولا معتبر بإعراب الواحدة عند عامة المشايخ، وهو الصحيح؛ لأن العوام لا يميزون بين وجوه الإعراب. اهـ

وقوله : فيها مقتضى، أو مضمر : يعني أن ثبوت الطلاق بهذه الألفاظ، إما بطريق الاقتضاء، كما في اعتدي، واستبرئي رحمك؛ لأن الطلاق ثبت شرعاً، لا لغة وإما بطريق الإضمار، كما في قوله: أنت واحدة؛ لأنه لما زال الإبهام بنية الطلاق: ثبت الطلاق لغةً على أنه مضمر فيه، بحذف الموصوف، وإقامة الصفة مقامه، وهذا شائع في كلامهم.

Intention and the Number of Divorces in Kinayah Utterances
Explaining the role of intention in determining the number of divorces from kinayah words

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband intends two or three divorces using a kinayah word, and explain why intending two results in only one.

Core Idea
Intention can make kinayah triple, but two defaults to one; the word cannot convey the number two.

Kinayah words require intention to effect divorce, but what if the husband intends a specific number? The text clarifies that if the husband intends three, it is three because the word can imply severe separation; if he intends two, it cannot, because the word does not indicate a number, so the minimum separation—one—is applied. This demonstrates the Hanafi commitment to literal probability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterance as kinayah
First confirm that the word is classified as a kinayah, not sarih. Words like 'bā'in', 'battlah', etc. are kinayah.
Sarih words like 'anti taliq' do not require intention.
2
Determine the husband's stated intention
What number did the husband claim to intend? The passage covers intending one, two, or three.
Intention is hidden, but in court it is based on his claim unless evidence contradicts.
3
Apply the rule for intention of three
If he intends three, it is three because the word can bear the meaning of severe separation (baynunah mughallazah).
This is based on the word's potential meaning.
4
Apply the rule for intention of two
If he intends two, it is only one because the word does not denote the number two; the lesser (one) is presumed.
The text says 'two is a pure number, no indication'.
5
Understand the underlying principle
Kinayah words are ambiguous, so the least probable meaning consistent with intention is taken, unless a specific higher meaning is intended and linguistically possible.
This is a key Hanafi usul principle in divorce.

(فإن لم تكن له نية الطلاق لم يقع بهذه الألفاظ طلاق)؛ لأنها تحتمله وغيره، والطلاق لا يقع بالاحتمال، إلا أن يكونا: أي الزوجان في مذاكرة الطلاق: فيقع بها الطلاق) أي: ببعضها، وهو: كل لفظ لا يصلح رداً لقولها. وهذا (في القضاء)؛ لأن الظاهر أن مراده الطلاق، والقاضي إنما يقضي بالظاهر. (وإن لم يكونا في مذاكرة الطلاق و) لكن (كانا في غضب، أو خصومة: وقع الطلاق قضاء أيضاً (بكل لفظ لا يُقصد به السَّبُّ، والشتيمة)؛ لأن الغضب يدل على إرادة الطلاق. (ولم يقع بما يُقصد به السب والشتيمة، إلا أن ينويه)؛ لأن الحال يدل على إرادة السب والشتيمة.
And if he intended by it three [divorces]: it is three, and if he intended two: it is one. And this is like his saying: 'You are bā'in', and 'bā'inah', and 'bayyinah', and 'battlah', and 'harām', and 'your rope is on your camel's hump', and 'join your family', and 'khaliyyah', and 'bariyyah', and 'I grant you to your family', and 'I released you', and 'I separated from you', and 'you are free', and 'cover yourself', and 'veil yourself', and 'conceal yourself', and 'be distant' - with a mu'jama then muhmala: from ghurba meaning distance, or 'be celibate' - with a muhmala then mu'jama: from 'uzūba meaning lack of a husband, or 'go out', or 'leave', or 'stand up', or 'seek husbands', or similar to that. And if he intended by it three divorces: it is three; because separation is of two types: severe, which is three, and light, which is one, so whichever he intended: it occurs due to the word's probability. And if he intended two, it is one divorce; because two is a pure number, and the word gives no indication of it; so the least of the two separations is established, which is one. But if he had no intention of divorce: divorce does not occur with these words, unless they are in a discussion of divorce: then divorce occurs by them in the court, and does not occur between him and Allah Almighty, unless he intended divorce by it. And if they are not in a discussion of divorce and were in anger or dispute: divorce occurs with every word not intended for insult and abuse, and does not occur with that which is intended for insult and abuse, unless he intends it. And this (in the court); because the apparent is that his intent is divorce, and the judge only judges by the apparent. (And it does not occur with that which is suitable as a reply to her statement; due to the probability of intending the reply, and it is the lower [probability], so it is attributed to it, and not between him and Allah Almighty) in all cases, unless he intends divorce by it); because it is probable for other things. (And if they are not in a discussion of divorce and) but (they were in anger or dispute: divorce occurs in the court as well (with every word not intended for abuse and insult); because anger indicates the desire for divorce. (And it does not occur with that which is intended for insult and abuse, unless he intends it); because the situation indicates the desire for insult and abuse.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage from Al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Quduri addresses the rulings on kinayah (ambiguous) words of divorce. The text begins by stating that if a husband intends triple divorce when using such a word, it counts as three; if he intends two, it counts as only one. The reason given in the commentary (sharh) is that separation (baynunah) is of two types: severe (triple) and light (single), and since the word does not specifically denote two, the lesser is established. The passage then lists many common kinayah words, such as 'bā'in', 'bā'inah', 'battlah', 'harām', etc., explaining that these words are ambiguous and depend on intention. The commentary further clarifies the conditions when intention is absent: If the husband had no specific intention of divorce, the divorce does not occur—unless the spouses were discussing divorce (mudhakarat al-talaq), in which case it occurs only in court (qada) due to the apparent context, but not in the sight of Allah unless the husband actually intended it. If they were in anger or a dispute, the ruling is more nuanced: divorce occurs with words that are not normally used for insult (like 'you are free' or 'go out') because anger indicates a desire to divorce, but it does not occur with words that are typically used for insults (like 'you are such-and-such') unless the husband intended divorce. This shows the Hanafi emphasis on intention and context in interpreting ambiguous utterances, a key principle in fiqh al-kinayah.

Key Terms
كناية (kināyah): An ambiguous word or phrase that can imply divorce but is not explicitly clear, requiring intention or context to determine its effect.
بائن (bāʼin): Literally 'separated'; a kinayah word for divorce. When intended, it results in a bain (irrevocable) divorce.
مذاكرة الطلاق (mudhākarat al-ṭalāq): The context where spouses are discussing divorce, which makes kinayah words effective in court even without explicit intention.
قضاء (qaḍāʼ): The legal ruling in court based on apparent circumstances, as opposed to the actual reality between the person and Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) One
C) Two
D) Three

Key Takeaway

The Effect of Context on Kinayah Divorce: Discussion, Anger, and the Qada/Diyanah Distinction
Explaining the effect of context (divorce discussion, anger) on kinayah divorce and the qada/diyanah distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a kinayah divorce takes effect in court (qada) and when it takes effect between the husband and Allah (diyanah), based on the contexts of divorce discussion and anger.

Core Idea
In court, context determines divorce; before Allah, only intention.

The previous lesson focused on the husband's intention regarding the number of divorces. But what if the husband has no specific intention? The source now discusses the effect of external contexts: if the couple is discussing divorce, certain words trigger a divorce in court even without intention, because the situation implies it. Similarly, if they are angry, words not typically used as insults are treated as divorce in court. However, between the husband and Allah, nothing occurs unless he actually intended it. This distinction between qada (court ruling) and diyanah (religious reality) is a hallmark of Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Determine if the spouses are in a context of divorce discussion (mudhakarat al-talaq)
If they were discussing divorce, then kinayah words that are not suitable as a reply to her statement are treated as divorce in court.
Words that could be a reply (like 'be distant' if she said 'I'm leaving') are excluded.
2
Identify the ruling in court (qada) for mudhakarat
Divorce occurs in court because the apparent indicates intent. But between him and Allah, it only occurs if he actually intended divorce.
The judge relies on the apparent.
3
Analyze the context of anger or dispute
If the couple is angry or in dispute, divorce occurs in court with every word that is not customarily used for insult or abuse.
Anger is seen as a strong indicator of desire to divorce.
4
Distinguish between words of divorce and words of insult
Words like 'you are free', 'go out' are not insults, so divorce occurs in court. Words like 'you are a donkey' are insults and do not cause divorce unless intended.
The situation indicates the purpose.
5
Understand the qada/diyanah distinction across contexts
In all contexts, the external ruling (qada) is based on apparent indicators, but the religious reality (diyanah) depends on actual intention. This is a fundamental principle.
It protects the marriage from unintended consequences when the husband was not serious.

Ruling of Implicit Divorce Without Intention and in Conversation About Divorce
Explains the ruling on implicit divorce without intention and the exception for mudhaakarat in court.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on implicit divorce (kinayah) when the husband has no intention, and explain the exception of mudhaakarat al-talaq for court rulings.

Core Idea
Implicit divorce requires intention; but in a divorce conversation, court presumes intention, though Allah's ruling still requires it.

This lesson explores a foundational Hanafi principle: that implicit divorce (kinayah) does not take effect without clear intention. However, the context of a divorce conversation (mudhaakarat al-talaq) creates an exception in court, where the apparent circumstances override the lack of stated intention. We will unpack how the judge rules based on external signs, and how this contrasts with what is valid between the husband and Allah.

Guided Path
1
Recall the general rule
Start with the core principle: implicit divorce words (like 'You are free') require the husband's intention to divorce. Without it, no divorce occurs because the words are ambiguous and probability does not suffice.
2
Identify the exception
The text introduces an exception: if the spouses are in a conversation specifically about divorce (mudhaakarat al-talaq), then in court, divorce does occur even without inner intention, because the apparent context indicates the husband's intent.
Mudhaakarat means they are discussing divorce right then.
3
Distinguish court ruling from internal ruling
The judge rules based on apparent circumstances (zahir). So in court, divorce is effective. But between the husband and Allah, it is not a divorce unless he actually intended it. This dual ruling is a distinctive Hanafi feature.
4
Apply the qualification for words suitable as a response
Not all implicit words in mudhaakarat cause divorce in court. Only those that cannot be understood as a reply to the wife's statement. If the word could be a response, it is presumed he meant the response, not divorce.
Example: if wife says 'You are divorced', husband says 'You are free' – this may be a response, not a divorce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن لم تكن له نية الطلاق: لم يقع بهذه الألفاظ طلاق، إلا أن يكونا في مذاكرة الطلاق: فيقع بها الطلاق في القضاء، ولا يقع فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى، إلا أن ينوي به الطلاق. (فإن لم تكن له نية الطلاق لم يقع بهذه الألفاظ طلاق)؛ لأنها تحتمله وغيره، والطلاق لا يقع بالاحتمال، إلا أن يكونا): أي الزوجان في مذاكرة الطلاق: فيقع بها الطلاق) أي: ببعضها، وهو: كل لفظ لا يصلح رداً لقولها. (وهذا في القضاء)؛ لأن الظاهر أن مراده الطلاق، والقاضي إنما يقضي بالظاهر. (ولا يقع فيما يصلح رداً لقولها؛ لاحتمال إرادة الرد، وهو الأدنى، فيُحمل عليه، ولا فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى) في الجميع، إلا أن ينوي به الطلاق)؛ لأنه يحتمل غيره.
If he has no intention of divorce, divorce does not occur by these words, unless they are in conversation about divorce: then divorce occurs by them in court, and it does not occur between him and Allah Almighty, unless he intends divorce by them. (If he has no intention of divorce, divorce does not occur by these words) because they are ambiguous and can mean other things, and divorce does not occur by probability, unless they are): i.e., the two spouses are in conversation about divorce: then divorce occurs by them) i.e., by some of them, which is every word that is not suitable as a response to her words. (And this is in court) because the apparent [circumstances] indicate that his intention is divorce, and the judge only judges by the apparent. (And it does not occur by what is suitable as a response to her words; due to the possibility of intending a response, which is the lower [meaning], so it is attributed to it, and not between him and Allah Almighty) in all cases, unless he intends divorce by it) because it could mean other things.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the matn and its commentary. It addresses the case where a husband pronounces an implicit expression of divorce (kinayah) without any intention. The general rule is that no divorce takes effect because the words are ambiguous and divorce cannot be based on probability. However, an exception arises when the spouses are engaged in a conversation specifically about divorce (mudhaakarat al-talaq). In that context, the apparent circumstance indicates the husband intends divorce, so the divorce is effective in court (qada'), even if he privately did not intend it. Nevertheless, between him and Allah, it is not considered a divorce unless he actually intended it. The commentary further qualifies that only implicit words that cannot be taken as a response to the wife's speech cause divorce in court; if the word is suitable as a response (e.g., replying to her statement), it is presumed he meant the response, not divorce. The judge bases his ruling on the apparent meaning.

Key Terms
مذاكرة الطلاق (mudhaakarat al-talaq): The spouses being in a conversation specifically about divorce.
القضاء (al-qada'): The ruling of the court based on apparent circumstances.
الاحتمال (al-ihtimal): Ambiguity or probability; divorce does not occur based on mere probability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
