Hanafi Ruling on Implicit Divorce During Anger: Non-Insulting vs Insulting Words
States that during anger, divorce occurs by non-insulting words but not by insulting words unless intended.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on implicit divorce words uttered in anger and distinguish between words that are typically insulting and those that are not, explaining why the context of anger affects the ruling.

Core Idea
In anger, non-insulting words trigger divorce; insulting words do not — context reveals intent.

This lesson explores a nuanced Hanafi ruling regarding implicit divorce (talāq bi al-kināyah) when the husband is angry or quarreling. The passage from al-Lubāb distinguishes between words that are commonly used as insults and those that are not. The key insight is that anger itself becomes a contextual indicator (qarīna) that clarifies what the husband intended. Understanding this rule requires careful attention to the type of word used and the surrounding circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The spouses are not in a conversation about divorce but are in a state of anger or quarrel. This context sets the stage for interpreting ambiguous words.
The scenario excludes cases where divorce is being discussed directly.
2
Categorise the word
Determine whether the word used by the husband is typically an insult/curse (sabb wa shatimah) or not. This classification is crucial for the ruling.
The text divides words into two categories based on common usage.
3
Apply the ruling for non-insulting words
If the word is not typically insulting, divorce occurs in court (qada'an) even without explicit intention, because anger indicates a desire for divorce.
Anger serves as an external evidence (qarīna) for divorce intention.
4
Apply the ruling for insulting words
If the word is typically insulting, divorce does not occur unless the husband explicitly intends divorce. Anger here indicates a desire to insult, not divorce.
The context points to insult, so the word is taken as an insult, not divorce.
5
Understand the reasoning
The Hanafi school relies on typical human behaviour: in anger, a person may say things to end the relationship (non-insulting) or to vent (insulting). The nature of the word aligns with different intentions.
This ruling demonstrates the principle that external contexts (qarā'in) can clarify ambiguous speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن لم يكونا في مذاكرة الطلاق وكانا في غضب، أو خصومة: وقع الطلاق بكل لفظ لا يُقصد به السب والشتيمة، ولم يقع بما يُقصد به السب والشتيمة، إلا أن ينويه. (وإن لم يكونا في مذاكرة الطلاق و) لكن (كانا في غضب، أو خصومة: وقَعَ الطلاق قضاء أيضاً (بكل لفظ لا يُقصد به السَّبُّ، والشتيمة)؛ لأن الغضب يدل على إرادة الطلاق. (ولم يقع بما يُقصد به السب والشتيمة، إلا أن ينويه)؛ لأن الحال يدل على إرادة السب والشتيمة.
And if they are not in conversation about divorce, but are in anger or quarrel: divorce occurs by every word that is not intended as insult and curse, and does not occur by what is intended as insult and curse, unless he intends it. (And if they are not in conversation about divorce and) but (they are in anger or quarrel: divorce occurs in court also by every word that is not intended as insult and curse) because anger indicates desire for divorce. (And it does not occur by what is intended as insult and curse, unless he intends it) because the situation indicates desire for insult and curse.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling when spouses are not specifically discussing divorce but are in a state of anger or quarrel. The key factor is that anger indicates the husband likely intends to divorce, so if he uses a word that is not typically used as an insult or curse, the divorce takes effect in court (qada'), even without explicit inner intention. However, if he uses words that are commonly used for insult or curse, it is presumed he intended insult, not divorce, and thus no divorce occurs unless he explicitly intends divorce. The reasoning is that in anger, the situation clarifies the meaning: words of insult show the intent to insult, while words not originally insults show the intent to divorce.

Key Terms
السب والشتيمة (al-sabb wa al-shatimah): Insult and curse words, typically used to demean.
حالة الغضب (halat al-ghadab): The state of anger, a context that influences the interpretation of implicit divorce words.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Yes, because anger indicates divorce.

C) Yes, because any word can be divorce.
D) No, unless the wife agrees.

Key Takeaway
In anger, non-insulting kinayat cause divorce automatically; insulting words do not, unless the husband explicitly intends divorce.

Systematic Classification of States and Implicit Divorce Expressions (Kinayah)
Systematic breakdown of states and kinayah categories with rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any implicit divorce expression into its category and determine whether divorce occurs based on the husband's state.

Core Idea
Divorce by implicit words depends on two factors: the word's category and the husband's emotional state.

This passage provides the Hanafi framework for understanding how implicit (kinayah) divorce expressions operate. It categorizes both the husband's emotional state (calm, discussing divorce, anger) and the words themselves into three types. The ruling varies by combination, making it a highly contextual area of fiqh. Mastering this classification is essential for correctly applying Hanafi rulings on verbal divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three states
List the three states mentioned: halat al-rida (calm/satisfaction), halat mudhaakarat al-talaq (conversation about divorce), and halat al-ghadab (anger). Each state carries a different legal presumption.
The state is determined by the husband's emotional condition and external context at the time of utterance.
2
Identify the three categories of kinayah
Classify each implicit word into one of three categories based on its suitability for answer (jawab), response (radd), or insult (shatm). Category 1: suitable only for answer. Category 2: suitable for answer and insult. Category 3: suitable for answer and response.
Each category has a list of specific example words; synonyms are treated similarly.
3
Determine which categories are effective in each state
Apply the rule: in calm, no category is effective without explicit intention. In conversation, categories 1 and 2 are effective (words not suitable for response). In anger, only category 1 is effective (words not suitable for insult or response).
The key principle is whether the word can be plausibly interpreted as a response or insult given the state.
4
Apply to a given scenario
Given a word and a state, identify the word's category and then apply the rule to determine if divorce occurs. For calm, intention is also needed. For the other states, intention is not required for the effective categories.
The husband's word is taken regarding lack of intention in the calm state (yusaddaq bi qaydihi).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيان ذلك: أن الأحوال ثلاثة : حالة مطلقة، الرضا، وحالة مذاكرة الطلاق، وحالة الغضب. والكنايات ثلاثة أقسام: قسم منها يصلح جواباً، ولا يصلح رداً، ولا شتماً، ألفاظ : أمرك بيدك اختاري اعتدي، ومرادفها. وقسم يصلح جواباً وشتماً، ولا يصلح رداً ، خمسة ألفاظ : وهي خلية، بَرية، بتة، بائن، حرام، ومرادفها. وقسم يصلح جواباً ورداً، ولا يصلح سباً وشتماً، أيضاً: اخرجي، اذهبي، اغربي، قومي، تقنَّعي، ومرادفها. ففي حالة الرضا : لا يقع الطلاق بشيء منها إلا بالنية، والقولُ قوله في عدم النية. وفي حالة مذاكرة الطلاق يقع بكل لفظ لا يصلح للرد، وهو القسم الأول والثاني. وفي حالة الغضب : لا يقع بكل لفظ يصلح للسب والرد، وهو القسم الثاني والثالث، ويقع بكل لفظ لا يصلح لهما، بل للجواب فقط، وهو القسم الأول، كما في «الإيضاح».
And the explanation of that: The states are three: the state of absolute, satisfaction, and the state of conversation about divorce, and the state of anger. And the implicit expressions are three categories: A category that is suitable as an answer, not suitable as a response, nor as insult; words: 'Your matter is in your hand', 'choose', 'i'taddi', and their synonyms. And a category that is suitable as an answer and insult, not suitable as a response; five words: khaliyyah, barriyyah, battah, ba'in, haram, and their synonyms. And a category that is suitable as an answer and response, not suitable as insult and curse; also: 'ukhruji', 'idhhabi', 'ughrubi', 'qumi', 'taqanna'i', and their synonyms. So in the state of satisfaction: divorce does not occur by any of them except with intention, and his statement is taken regarding the lack of intention. And in the state of conversation about divorce, it occurs by every word that is not suitable for response, which is the first and second category. And in the state of anger: it does not occur by every word that is suitable for insult and response, which is the second and third category, and it occurs by every word that is not suitable for them, but only for answer, which is the first category, as in 'al-Idah'.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage systematically summarizes the rules of implicit divorce (kinayat) by linking three states to three categories of words. The three states are: normal calm (halat al-rida or absolute state), state of discussing divorce (mudhaakarat al-talaq), and state of anger. The three categories of kinayah: (1) words that can be an answer (jawab) but not a response (radd) nor insult: e.g., 'amruki biyadik' (your affair is in your hand), 'ikhtari' (choose), 'i'taddi' (count your 'iddah); (2) words that are answer and insult, not response: five words: khaliyyah (free), barriyyah (free), battah (absolutely), ba'in (separated), haram (forbidden); (3) words that are answer and response but not insult: e.g., 'ukhruji' (leave), 'idhhabi' (go), 'ughrubi' (go away), 'qumi' (stand up), 'taqanna'i' (cover yourself). The ruling: In the calm state, no divorce occurs with any without explicit intention, and the husband's word is trusted regarding lack of intention. In the conversation state, divorce occurs with categories 1 and 2 (words that are not suitable as response). In anger, divorce does not occur with categories 2 and 3 (words suitable as insult or response), but occurs with category 1 (words only suitable as answer). This key summary ties the earlier specific rulings into a coherent system.

Key Terms
حالة الرضا (halat al-rida): The state of calm and satisfaction, i.e., normal circumstances without anger or prior discussion of divorce.
القسم الأول (al-qism al-awwal): First category of kinayah: words that serve as an answer (jawab) only, not for response (radd) or insult.
القسم الثاني (al-qism al-thani): Second category: words that serve as answer and insult but not response; includes five specific words.
القسم الثالث (al-qism al-thalith): Third category: words that serve as answer and response but not insult; includes commands to leave, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Divorce by kinayah in the Hanafi school is determined by a matrix of three states and three word categories, with intention required only in the calm state.

Kinayat al-Talaq: Categories and States
Classifies kinayat and their rulings based on context

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three categories of kinayat and state whether divorce occurs in each of the three states, with or without intention.

Core Idea
The effect of a kinayah depends on the husband's state and the word's suitability for rejection or insult.

In Hanafi law, divorce can be effected by explicit words (sarih) that require no intention, or by implied words (kinayah) whose effect depends on context. This lesson classifies the types of kinayah and the three states of the husband—satisfaction, discussing divorce, and anger—and explains when intention is needed for divorce to occur. Mastering this classification is essential for correctly applying the rules of talaq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three states
The text mentions three states: satisfaction (rida), discussing divorce (mudhakarat al-talaq), and anger (ghadab). Each affects the ruling differently.
State is a condition that modifies the legal effect of the utterance.
2
Identify the three categories of kinayat
The text divides kinayat into three groups based on whether they are suitable as a response, rejection, or insult. Note the examples for each category.
First category: only response. Second: response and insult. Third: response and rejection.
3
Map each state to the categories that effect divorce
In satisfaction, all categories require intention. In discussing divorce, categories that cannot be rejected (first and second) effect divorce without intention. In anger, categories that can be insult or rejection (second and third) do not effect divorce; only the first category does.
The key principle: words that can be taken as rejection or insult are not effective in anger or discussion contexts, respectively.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق
بيان ألفاظ الكنايات وأقسامها وأحوال النطق بها:
وبيان ذلك: أن الأحوال ثلاثة: حالة الرضا، وحالة مذاكرة الطلاق، وحالة الغضب.
والكنايات ثلاثة أقسام:
قسم منها يصلح جواباً، ولا يصلح رداً، ولا شتماً، ألفاظ: أمرك بيدك اختاري اعتدي، ومرادفها.
وقسم يصلح جواباً وشتماً، ولا يصلح رداً، خمسة ألفاظ: خلية، بَريَّة، بتة، بائن، حرام، ومرادفها.
وقسم يصلح جواباً ورداً، ولا يصلح سباً وشتماً، أيضاً: اخرجي، اذهبي، اغربي، قومي، تقنَّعي، ومرادفها.
ففي حالة الرضا: لا يقع الطلاق بشيء منها إلا بالنية، والقولُ قوله في عدم النية.
وفي حالة مذاكرة الطلاق يقع بكل لفظ لا يصلح للرد، وهو القسم الأول والثاني.
وفي حالة الغضب: لا يقع بكل لفظ يصلح للسب والرد، وهو القسم الثاني والثالث، ويقع بكل لفظ لا يصلح لهما، بل للجواب فقط، وهو القسم الأول، كما في «الإيضاح».
Book of Divorce. Explanation of the words of kinayat, their categories, and the states of uttering them. The explanation is that the states are three: the state of satisfaction, the state of discussing divorce, and the state of anger. The kinayat are three categories: A category that is suitable as a response, not suitable as a rejection, nor as an insult; its words are: 'your affair is in your hand', 'choose', 'count yourself in iddah', and their synonyms. A category that is suitable as a response and an insult, not suitable as a rejection; five words: khaliyyah, barriyyah, battah, ba'in, haram, and their synonyms. A category that is suitable as a response and a rejection, not suitable as a curse and insult; also: 'leave', 'go', 'depart', 'stand up', 'cover yourself', and their synonyms. In the state of satisfaction: divorce does not occur by any of them except with intention, and his word is accepted regarding lack of intention. In the state of discussing divorce: it occurs by every word not suitable for rejection, which is the first and second categories. In the state of anger: it does not occur by every word suitable for insult and rejection, which is the second and third categories, and it occurs by every word not suitable for both, but only for a response, which is the first category, as in 'al-Idah'.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage defines the types of kinayat (metaphorical expressions used for divorce) and their legal effect depending on the husband's state. The author first states that there are three states: 1) satisfaction (rida) — when the husband is content and not discussing divorce, 2) discussing divorce (mudhakarat al-talaq) — when the couple is talking about divorce, and 3) anger (ghadab) — when the husband is angry. Then the kinayat are divided into three categories based on their suitability for different contexts: the first category (words like 'your affair is in your hand') is suitable only as a response to a question; the second category (words like 'khaliyyah') is suitable as a response and an insult; the third category (words like 'leave') is suitable as a response and a rejection. The ruling: in the state of satisfaction, divorce only occurs if the husband intended it; if he claims no intention, his word is accepted. In the state of discussing divorce, divorce occurs by the first two categories (since they cannot be rejected). In the state of anger, divorce does not occur by words that can be insult or rejection (categories two and three), but it does occur by the first category (which is only suitable as a response). This is a Hanafi ruling as found in Al-Idah.

Key Terms
كناية (kinayah): An expression that implies divorce indirectly, not by the explicit words 'talaq' or 'firaq'.
حالة الرضا (halat al-rida): The state where the husband is satisfied and not discussing divorce; kinayat require intention.
حالة مذاكرة الطلاق (halat mudhakarat al-talaq): The state where the couple is talking about divorce; kinayat that are not suitable for rejection effect divorce even without intention.
حالة الغضب (halat al-ghadab): The state where the husband is angry; kinayat that can be insults or rejections do not effect divorce.

Key Takeaway
The legal effect of a kinayah is not fixed; it depends on the husband's state and the word's suitability for rejection or insult.

Describing Divorce with Severity Makes it Irrevocable (Bain)
Ruling on talaq with added descriptive words of severity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a husband adds descriptive words of severity to talaq and identify the resulting type and number of divorces based on his intention.

Core Idea
Describing divorce with words like 'bain' or 'most severe' makes it irrevocable (bain) immediately, regardless of whether more than one was intended.

When a husband utters the word 'talaq' and adds descriptive terms that indicate severity or finality, the divorce becomes irrevocable (bain) instantly. This lesson explains which phrases trigger this ruling and how the husband's intention affects the number of divorces that occur.

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterance
Consider the exact words the husband uses. If he says 'talaq' alone, it may be raj'i. The addition of descriptive words like 'bain' or 'ashadd al-talaq' changes the ruling.
2
Apply the ruling on descriptions
According to the Hanafi school, any description that indicates severity or intensity makes the talaq bain. The description conveys a meaning beyond the simple word, namely immediate and irrevocable separation.
Examples: 'bain', 'ashadd al-talaq', 'afhash al-talaq', 'talaq al-shaytan', etc.
3
Determine the number based on intention
If the husband has no specific intention, it counts as one bain talaq. If he intends two (and the wife is free), it becomes two bain. If he intends three, it becomes three bain.
The intention is considered when there is ambiguity about the number.
4
Handle separate utterances
If the husband says 'you are divorced one' and then separately says 'bain' or 'al-battah' intending another divorce, two bain divorces occur because the description can initiate a new pronouncement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا وَصَفَ الطلاق بضَرْب من الزيادة والشدة: كان بائناً؛ لأن الطلاق يقع بمجرد اللفظ، فإذا وَصَفَه بزيادة وشدة: أفاد معنى ليس في لفظه. وذلك مثل أن يقول: أنت طالق بائن، أو: طالق أشد الطلاق، أو أفحش الطلاق، أو أشَرَّه، أو أخبثه، أو طلاق الشيطان، أو البدعة، أو كالجبل أو ملء البيت، أو: عريضه، أو طويله؛ لأن الطلاق إنما يوصف بهذه الصفة باعتبار أثره، وهي البينونة في الحال، فتقع واحدةً بائنة إذا لم يكن له نية، أو نوى ثنتين في غير الأمة، أما إذا نوى الثلاث فثلاث؛ لما مر من قبل. ولو عنى بقوله: أنت طالق واحدة وبقوله: بائن، أو البتة أخرى: يقع تطليقتان بائنتان؛ لأن هذا الوصف يصلح لابتداء الإيقاع. «هداية». اختلفت نسخ القدوري هنا، ففي بعضها: وفي بعضها: «أو».
And if one describes divorce with a kind of increase and severity: it is bain (irrevocable). Because divorce occurs by mere utterance, so if he describes it with increase and severity, it conveys a meaning not in its literal word. That is, such as his saying: 'You are divorced bain', or 'divorced with the most severe divorce', or 'the foulest divorce', or 'the worst divorce', or 'the most filthy', or 'the devil's divorce', or 'the bid'ah divorce', or 'like a mountain', or 'filling the house', or 'its width', or 'its length'. Because divorce is only described with this quality with regard to its effect, which is immediate irrevocability. So it occurs as one bain pronouncement if he has no intention [of more], or if he intends two [in the case of] a non-slave woman, but if he intends three, then three; as has preceded. And if he meant by his saying: 'You are divorced one' and by his saying: 'bain' or 'al-battah' another: two bain pronouncements occur; because this description is suitable for initiating the occurrence. [From] Hidayah. The copies of al-Quduri differ here: in some it is [omitted] and in some it is 'or'.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage explains the effect when a husband accompanies the word 'talaq' with additional descriptions that indicate severity or intensity. The ruling is that such a divorce is bain (irrevocable) immediately. The reasoning is that divorce occurs by the mere utterance of the word talaq itself; when the husband adds descriptions like 'bain', 'most severe', etc., he is adding a meaning not present in the simple word. Since these words describe the effect—immediate separation—the divorce becomes bain. The examples given include 'ashadd al-talaq', 'afhash al-talaq', 'talaq al-shaytan', 'al-bid'ah', 'ka al-jabal', 'mil' al-bayt', etc. If the husband has no intention of a specific number, it counts as one bain divorce. If he intends two (and the wife is not a slave), it becomes two bain divorces. If he intends three, then three. However, if he says 'you are divorced one' and then adds 'bain' or 'al-battah' as a separate utterance meaning another divorce, two bain divorces occur. The source is al-Hidayah. The author also notes a variant in Quduri's text regarding the letter 'aw' (or).

Key Terms
بائن (ba'in): Irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately without the right of return except through a new marriage.
أشد الطلاق (ashadd al-talaq): A phrase used to describe talaq as the most severe, which makes it bain.
البتة (al-battah): A term meaning 'definitively', used in talaq to indicate irrevocability.

Describing Divorce with Severity Makes it Ba'in
Ruling on adding harsh descriptions to divorce pronouncement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that describing divorce with severe words makes the divorce ba'in and determine the number of divorces based on intention.

Core Idea
When divorce is described with harsh words, the divorce becomes irrevocable (ba'in) immediately, but the count remains one unless more was intended.

The Hanafi school teaches that the wording of divorce matters. If a husband adds words of severity or increase, the divorce takes an immediate irrevocable effect. However, the number of divorces—one, two, or three—depends entirely on his intention at the time. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its application through examples from the classical text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the descriptive wording
First, recognize phrases like 'bāʾin', 'severest divorce', 'foulest divorce', 'like a mountain', etc. These are the 'descriptions with severity' that trigger the ba'in effect.
The key is that the description goes beyond the simple word 'divorced'.
2
Determine the husband's intent
Next, establish what the husband intended by his utterance. Did he mean one divorce, two, or three? This is crucial because the count follows his intention.
If no intention is expressed, the default is one ba'in divorce.
3
Apply the intention-based count
Using the intention, apply the rule: intention for one → one ba'in; intention for two (for a free woman) → two ba'in; intention for three → three ba'in. If the description is followed by a separate count phrase (e.g., 'irrevocably' meaning another), then additional divorces may occur.
The description itself does not increase the count; only intention does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا وَصَفَ الطلاق بضَرْب من الزيادة والشدة: كان بائناً، مثل أن يقول: أنت طالق بائن، أو: طالق أشدَّ الطلاق، أو: أفحش الطلاق، أو: طلاق الشيطان، أو: البدعة، أو: كالجبل، أو: ملء البيت .
وصف الطلاق بالشدة :] (وإذا وَصَفَ الطلاق بضَرْب من الزيادة والشدة: كان) الطلاق (بائنا)؛ لأن الطلاق يقع بمجرد اللفظ، فإذا وَصَفَه بزيادة وشدة: أفاد معنى ليس في لفظه.
وذلك (مثل أن يقول: أنت طالق بائن، أو: طالق أشد الطلاق، أو أفحش الطلاق، أو أشَرَّه، أو أخبثه أو طلاق الشيطان، أو البدعة، أو كالجبل أو ملء البيت، أو: عريضه، أو طويله؛ لأن الطلاق إنما يوصف بهذه الصفة باعتبار أثره، وهي البينونة في الحال، فتقع واحدةً بائنة إذا لم يكن له نية، أو نوى ثنتين في غير الأمة، أما إذا نوى الثلاث فثلاث؛ لما مر من قبل.
ولو عنى بقوله: أنت طالق واحدة وبقوله: بائن، أو البتة أخرى: يقع تطليقتان بائنتان؛ لأن هذا الوصف يصلح لابتداء الإيقاع.

And if he describes the divorce with a type of increase and severity, it becomes irrevocable (ba'in). For example, saying: 'You are divorced irrevocably,' or 'divorced with the severest divorce,' or 'the foulest divorce,' or 'the divorce of Satan,' or 'the innovated divorce,' or 'like a mountain,' or 'filling the house.'
[Commentary:] The description of divorce with severity: (And if he describes the divorce with a type of increase and severity, the divorce becomes) irrevocable (ba'in); because divorce occurs by virtue of the bare utterance, so if he describes it with increase and severity, it conveys a meaning not in the utterance itself.
That is (like his saying: 'You are divorced irrevocably,' or 'divorced with the severest divorce,' or 'the foulest divorce,' or 'the worst,' or 'the most evil,' or 'the divorce of Satan,' or 'the innovated divorce,' or 'like a mountain' or 'filling the house,' or 'wide,' or 'long'; because divorce is described with these attributes with regard to its effect, which is immediate irrevocability, so it occurs as one irrevocable divorce if he had no intention, or if he intended two in the case of a non-slave woman; but if he intended three, then three, as has preceded.
And if he meant by his statement 'You are divorced' one divorce and by his statement 'irrevocably' or 'absolutely' another, two irrevocable divorces occur; because this description is suitable for initiating the occurrence.
[Hidayah].
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage from Al-Lubab Sharh Al-Quduri explains the ruling on divorce pronounced with additional words indicating severity or increase. The core ruling: such descriptive words do not change the basic count of divorce (one) unless the husband had a specific intention for more. The divorce becomes ba'in (irrevocable) immediately due to the severity of the description, but the number of divorces is determined by intention. If no intention, one ba'in divorce occurs. If he intended two (and the wife is not a slave), two occur; if three, three occur. The commentary also addresses the case where the husband says 'You are divorced' meaning one, and then says 'irrevocably' meaning another — two separate divorces occur. This is because the additional description can itself initiate a new count. The Hidayah is cited as a source.

Key Terms
بائن (bāʾin): A type of divorce that is irrevocable; the husband cannot take back his wife without a new marriage contract.
البتة (al-battah): An expression meaning 'absolutely' or 'irrevocably,' used in the context of divorce to emphasize finality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) One revocable divorce
C) One irrevocable (ba'in) divorce
D) No divorce occurs

Key Takeaway
When a husband describes divorce with severe words, the divorce becomes ba'in (irrevocable) immediately, but the number of divorces is determined by his intention: one if no intention, two if intended two (for free woman), three if intended three.

Pronouncing Divorce on a Part of the Woman's Body
Rulings on valid and invalid expressions of talaq referring to body parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between expressions that represent the whole woman (causing divorce) and specific limbs (not causing divorce), and explain the reasoning using qiyas with sale.

Core Idea
Divorce takes effect only when referring to the entire woman or an undivided part; specific limbs like hand or leg do not constitute divorce.

This passage from al-Lubab clarifies a critical aspect of talaq: which bodily references count as divorce and which do not. The Hanafi school distinguishes between expressions that represent the whole (such as 'your neck,' 'your body') and those that refer to specific limbs (like 'your hand' or 'your leg'). Understanding this distinction prevents invalid divorce pronouncements and ensures legal certainty. The ruling also extends to undivided fractions (e.g., half) by analogy to transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify valid expressions
List from the text the expressions that represent the whole woman: 'you are divorced,' 'your neck,' 'your throat,' 'your spirit,' 'your body,' 'your flesh,' 'your private part,' 'your face,' 'your head.' These are treated as if he said 'you are divorced.'
These are synecdoches.
2
Understand the principle of representation
The rule: if the husband uses a term that stands for the entire woman (jism, badan, etc.), the divorce occurs. The text says 'because these things express the entirety.'
3
Analyze the case of undivided part (juz' shā'iʿ)
If he says 'half of you' or 'a third of you is divorced,' the divorce occurs. Reasoning: an undivided part is a valid subject for transactions like sale by qiyas, so it is valid for divorce. But divorce is not divisible, so it applies to the whole woman.
This is an example of qiyas: comparing talaq to sale.
4
Distinguish specific limbs
If he says 'your hand' or 'your leg is divorced,' the divorce does not occur. Reason: the hand is not a locus for divorce; it is a specific part that does not represent the whole. The text compares it to saying 'your saliva' or 'your nail' which are clearly invalid.
5
Apply the rule to new examples
Based on the principle, any other specific limb (e.g., finger, nose) would also be invalid, whereas any expression that connotes the whole (e.g., 'your soul') would be valid.
This is an expansion via analogy.

Source Text

وإذا أضاف الطلاق إلى جملتها، أو إلى ما يعبر به عن الجملة: وقع الطلاق، مثل أن يقول: أنت طالق، أو: رَقَبتُك طالق، أو: عُنقك طالق، أو: روحُك طالقٌ، أو: جَسَدك، أو: بدنُك، أو: فَرْجُك، أو: وجهكِ .
وكذلك إن طلق جزءاً شائعاً منها، مثل أن يقول: نصفك، أو: ثلثك طالق.
وإن قال: يدكِ، أو: رِجْلُكِ طالق: لم يقع الطلاق .

(وإن قال: يَدُك، أو: رِجْلُك طالقٌ: لم يقع الطلاق)؛ لإضافته إلى غير محله؛ فيلغو، كما إذا أضافه إلى ريقها، أو إلى ظُفُرها.
And if he adds the divorce to her entirety, or to that which expresses the entirety, the divorce occurs. For example, saying: 'You are divorced,' or 'Your neck is divorced,' or 'Your throat is divorced,' or 'Your spirit is divorced,' or 'Your body,' or 'Your flesh,' or 'Your private part,' or 'Your face.'
Similarly, if he divorces an undivided part of her, such as saying: 'Half of you,' or 'A third of you is divorced.'
And if he says: 'Your hand,' or 'Your leg is divorced,' the divorce does not occur.
[Commentary:] Adding divorce to the entire woman or an undivided part: (And if he adds the divorce to her entirety, or to that which expresses the entirety, the divorce occurs.) That is, like his saying to her: (You are divorced, or your neck is divorced, or your throat is divorced, or your spirit is divorced, or your body, or your flesh, or your private part, or your face, or your head; because these things express the entirety, so it is like his saying: 'You are divorced.'
(Similarly, if he divorces an undivided part of her), that is, like his saying to her: (Half of you, or a third of you is divorced); because an undivided part is a locus for various transactions like sale etc., so it is a locus for divorce, except that it is not divisible in terms of divorce, so it takes effect on the whole by necessity.
(And if he says: Your hand, or your leg is divorced: the divorce does not occur); because it is added to a non-locus, so it is invalid, just as if he added it to her saliva, or to her nail.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage explains the rules when a husband pronounces divorce by referring to the wife's body parts. The general principle: if the husband refers to the wife's entire person or something that represents the whole (like neck, spirit, body), the divorce takes effect. Similarly, if he refers to an undivided fraction (half, third), the divorce also takes effect, but it applies to the whole woman because divorce cannot be partial. However, if he refers to a specific limb that does not represent the whole, like hand, leg, or even a part like saliva or nail, the divorce does not occur at all. The reasoning is that such specific limbs are not considered the legal subject of divorce in themselves, so the pronouncement is null and void.

Key Terms
جزء شائع (juzʾ shāʾiʿ): An undivided or joint part of a whole, such as half or a third, that is not physically distinguished.
رقبة (raqabah): Literally 'neck,' used metonymically to refer to the entire person or slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Divorce occurs only when the husband's expression refers to the entire woman or an undivided part; specific limbs do not produce divorce.

Attaching Divorce to the Whole Woman or a Common Share
Ruling on divorce when attached to whole woman or common share

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorce when attached to expressions of the whole woman or a common share, and distinguish which body parts produce a valid divorce.

Core Idea
Divorce attached to any part that represents the whole woman is valid; a specific limb like 'hand' is not.

The Hanafi school has a detailed position on divorce wording. The key is whether the expression refers to the entire woman figuratively or to a specific part. This lesson will clarify which words work and why, drawing from al-Quduri and al-Maydani's commentary.

Guided Path
1
Identify expressions that represent the entirety
List the words that the text says are valid: neck, spirit, body, flesh, vagina, face, head. Recognise that these are used figuratively for the whole person.
2
Understand the rationale
The reason is that these words 'express the entirety' – they are not specific parts but are culturally understood to refer to the whole woman. Hence, saying 'your neck is divorced' is equivalent to 'you are divorced'.
3
Distinguish common share from specific limb
A common share (half, third) is also valid because it is a fraction of the whole, not a specific limb. Divorce, being indivisible, then applies to the entire woman.
Specific limbs like hand or foot are not common shares and do not represent the whole.
4
Apply the rule: invalid if attached to a non-locus
If the husband says 'your hand is divorced' or 'your foot is divorced', no divorce occurs because the expression is not a locus for divorce. It is akin to saying 'your saliva is divorced' – a null statement.
The ruling is based on whether the word is understood as representing the whole.

Source Text

And if he attaches divorce to her entirety, or to that which expresses the entirety, divorce occurs, such as saying: You are divorced, or your neck is divorced, or your spirit is divorced, or your body is divorced, or your flesh is divorced, or your vagina is divorced, or your face. Similarly, if he divorces a common share of her, such as saying: half of you, or a third of you is divorced. And if he says: your hand, or your foot is divorced, divorce does not occur. Adding divorce to the woman's entirety or to a common share: (And if he attaches divorce to her entirety, or to that which expresses the entirety, divorce occurs) such as saying to her: You are divorced, or your neck is divorced, or your spirit is divorced, or your body is divorced, or your flesh is divorced, or your vagina is divorced, or your face, or your head; because these things express the entirety; so it is like his saying: You are divorced. (And similarly if he divorces a common share of her) such as saying to her: half of you, or a third of you is divorced; because the common share is a locus for all transactions like sale and others, so it is a locus for divorce, but it is not divisible with respect to divorce, so it applies to the whole necessarily. (And if he says: your hand, or your foot is divorced, divorce does not occur) because he attached it to a non-locus; so it is void, as if he attached it to her saliva or her fingernail.

Commentary
This passage from al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Quduri deals with the validity of divorce when the wording is addressed to the woman's entire person or to a defined part. The matn states the rulings, and the sharh provides the reasoning. The Hanafi position is that if the husband says 'you are divorced' or uses any expression that refers to the entire woman (e.g., 'your neck', 'your spirit', 'your body', 'your face', 'your head'), divorce takes effect because these terms figuratively represent the whole. Similarly, if he says 'half of you' or 'a third of you' (a common share), divorce is effective, but since divorce is indivisible, it applies to the whole woman. However, if he specifies a limb that is not a common share but a differentiated part, like 'your hand' or 'your foot', divorce does not occur because the expression is not taken as referring to the whole; it is as if he divorced her saliva or fingernail, which is null.

Key Terms
جملة (jumla): The entire person of the woman referred to by expressions like 'your neck' or 'your spirit'.
جزء شائع (juz' shā'i'): An undivided share, such as half or one-third, which is a fraction of the whole.
طلاق (talāq): Divorce; the dissolution of a marriage by the husband's pronouncement.

Divorcing a Fraction of a Divorce Counts as One Full Divorce
Ruling with proof via talil (reasoning).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fractional talaq and explain why it counts as one full divorce.

Core Idea
Divorce is indivisible — mentioning half or a third counts as one full talaq.

This concise passage from al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Quduri addresses a common scenario: a husband says 'half a divorce' or 'one-third of a divorce.' You might think it results in only a partial divorce, but Hanafi law treats it as one complete divorce. Why? Because divorce cannot be divided into fractions. Understanding this principle helps you grasp the indivisibility of certain legal acts in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the action
The husband pronounces a fractional number of divorces (e.g., half, third).
The utterance is clear even though it is a fraction.
2
State the ruling
According to the text, this counts as one full divorce.
The text says 'كانت تطليقة واحدة'.
3
Locate the rationale
The phrase 'لأن الطلاق لا يتجزأ' provides the illa: divorce is indivisible.
This is a legal principle, not a scriptural text.
4
Apply the legal maxim
Mentioning part of an indivisible thing is like mentioning the whole.
This maxim applies to other acts like freeing a slave.
5
Confirm no exceptions
The Hanafi school holds this as the established position with no intra-madhab difference.
The position is mufta bihi and from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن طلقها نصف تطليقة ، أو ثلث تطليقة : كانت تطليقةً واحدةً.
وإن طلقها نصف تطليقة ، أو ثلث تطليقة: كانت تطليقةً واحدة)؛ لأن الطلاق لا يتجزأ، وذكر بعض مالا يتجزأ : كذكر الكل.
And if he divorces her with half a divorce or one-third of a divorce, it is one complete divorce. And if he divorces her with half a divorce or one-third of a divorce, it is one complete divorce because divorce cannot be divided, and mentioning a part of something indivisible is like mentioning the whole.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This short passage from al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Quduri addresses the situation where a husband utters a fractional number of divorces, such as 'half a divorce' or 'one-third of a divorce.' The Hanafi ruling is that such a pronouncement counts as one full divorce (tawliqa). The reason is that divorce is legally indivisible—it cannot be partially applied. Thus, mentioning a fraction is treated as if the husband intended one complete divorce. This is based on the principle that what is indivisible is considered as a whole in legal effects.

Key Terms
تطليقة (tawliqa): A single act of divorce, considered as an indivisible unit.
لا يتجزأ (lā yatajazzā): Indivisible; cannot be divided into smaller parts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Saying half or a third of a divorce counts as one full divorce because divorce is legally indivisible.

Validity of Divorce by Coerced or Intoxicated Person
Ruling on divorce pronounced under coercion or intoxication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorce by a coerced person and distinguish between intoxication from wine/nabidh and from banj, explaining the intra-Hanafi development.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, divorce by a coerced or intoxicated person is valid, but intoxication from banj was initially an exception, later overridden by deterrence.

In this lesson, we explore a nuanced area of Hanafi family law: the validity of divorce pronounced under coercion or intoxication. The basic ruling is clear, but the case of narcotic intoxication reveals an intra-Hanafi shift from a consensus-based exception to a deterrent-based rule. Understanding this development highlights how later Hanafi scholars sometimes adjusted rulings to prevent abuse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling
The matn states that divorce of a coerced person (mukrah) and an intoxicated person (sakran) is valid (waqi'). This is the starting point.
Coercion includes threats of harm.
2
Distinguish between types of intoxication
Al-Yanabi' clarifies that 'intoxicated' here refers to intoxication from wine (khamr) or nabidh (date wine), not from banj (narcotic) or medicine. For banj/medicine, there is consensus that divorce does not occur.
This is the early understanding.
3
Observe the later shift
Al-Jawahir states that in the current era, divorce from banj intoxication is valid as a deterrent (zajran) to prevent people from abusing the exception. This is now the relied-upon fatwa.
The principle of deterrence overrides the earlier consensus.
4
Determine the operative ruling
For wine/nabidh, divorce is valid by consensus. For coercion, always valid. For banj, the later fatwa makes it valid. For medicine, the early view still holds (invalid) unless analogized to banj.
Students should note that the passage does not address the medicine case in the later view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطلاق المكره، والسكران : واقع .
وطلاق المكره والسكران واقع. قال في الينابيع»: يريد بالسكران الذي سكر من الخمر، أو من النبيذ، أما إذا سكر من البنج (۳) ، أو من الدواء: لا يقع طلاقه بالإجماع.
قال في الجواهر)) وفي هذا الزمان إذا سكر بالبنج : يقع طلاقه ؛
زَجْراً له، وعليه الفتوى. * ثم الطلاق بالسكر من الخمر واقع، سواء شربها طوعاً،
And the divorce of the coerced person and the intoxicated person is valid. And the divorce of the coerced and intoxicated is valid. He said in al-Yanabi': He means by the intoxicated person one who becomes intoxicated from wine or from nabidh; as for one who becomes intoxicated from banj (a narcotic) or from medicine, his divorce does not occur by consensus. He said in al-Jawahir: And in this time, if one becomes intoxicated from banj, his divorce occurs as a deterrent, and upon it is the fatwa. Then the divorce due to intoxication from wine is valid, whether he drank it voluntarily,
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage from al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Quduri deals with the validity of divorce pronounced by a person who is coerced (mukrah) or intoxicated (sakran). The matn states generally that such divorce is valid. However, the sharh clarifies the meaning of 'intoxicated' by citing al-Yanabi', which distinguishes between intoxication from wine or nabidh (date wine) and from banj (a narcotic plant) or medicine. By consensus, if intoxication is from banj or medicine, divorce does not occur. Then al-Jawahir is quoted to show that in the current era, if one is intoxicated from banj, divorce does occur as a deterrent measure (zajran lahu), and this is the basis for fatwa. The passage ends by reiterating that divorce from wine intoxication is valid whether the person drank willingly or not. This shows an intra-Hanafi development where later scholars adjusted the ruling for social deterrence.

Key Terms
مكره (mukrah): A person who is coerced under threat of harm to pronounce divorce.
سكران (sakrān): A person intoxicated by an intoxicant.
بنج (banj): A narcotic plant that induces intoxication or stupor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is invalid by consensus.
B) It is valid as a deterrent.
C) It is valid only if he drank voluntarily.
D) It is invalid because banj is not an intoxicant.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, divorce by coercion or intoxication is valid; the later fatwa on banj extends this for deterrence, overriding earlier consensus.

Fractional Divorce Counts as One Complete Divorce
Ruling that a fractional divorce is treated as one full divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fractional divorce and apply the maxim 'divorce is indivisible' to similar pronouncements.

Core Idea
Mentioning half a divorce is like mentioning the whole—divorce is indivisible.

Have you ever wondered what happens if someone tries to issue a fractional divorce? The Hanafi school has a clear answer: because talaq is an indivisible act, any fraction counts as one full divorce. In this lesson, we will examine the ruling from al-Quduri's matn and understand the legal principle behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the husband's statement
Recognize the pronouncement as a fraction of a divorce, e.g., 'half divorce' or 'one-third divorce'.
The exact wording may vary but must clearly reference a fraction.
2
Apply the ruling
According to the matn, any fractional pronouncement counts as one complete divorce (talaq).
This is the literal ruling from the text.
3
Understand the legal maxim
The reason given is that divorce is indivisible (la yatajazzā); mentioning a part of an indivisible thing is like mentioning the whole.
This is a definitive principle in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Recognize the consequence
The marriage is affected by one revocable divorce (unless a different number is specified elsewhere).
Fractional intent does not reduce the effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن طلقها نصف تطليقة، أو ثلث تطليقة: كانت تطليقةً واحدة؛ لأن الطلاق لا يتجزأ، وذكر بعض مالا يتجزأ: كذكر الكل.
If he divorces her half a divorce, or one third of a divorce, it is one divorce; because divorce is indivisible, and mentioning a part of what is indivisible is like mentioning the whole.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This is a ruling from the matn of al-Quduri. The author states that if a husband says 'I divorce you half a divorce' or 'one third of a divorce', it counts as one complete divorce. The reasoning is that divorce is indivisible; it cannot be split into fractions. Therefore, mentioning a fraction is treated as mentioning the entire divorce. This is a straightforward ruling with no ikhtilaf among the Hanafis.

Key Terms
تطليقة (tadliqah): One instance of divorce; a single pronouncement of talaq.
لا يتجزأ (la yatajazzā): Indivisible; the act cannot be split into parts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) None

Key Takeaway

Divorce by Coerced and Intoxicated Persons: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on validity of divorce when drinking was coerced/necessitous

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two Hanafi positions on the validity of divorce by a coerced or necessitous drinker and state the later fatwa on bang intoxication.

Core Idea
Divorce by a coerced or intoxicated person is generally effective, but a Hanafi dispute arises when the drinking itself was coerced or out of necessity.

The classical Hanafi matn states that the divorce of a coerced person (mukrah) and an intoxicated person (sakrān) is effective (wāqiʿ). However, the commentators refine this rule. The term 'intoxicated' is specified to mean intoxication from wine or nabīdh, not from bang or medicine—where there was consensus that divorce does not occur. Later fatwa, as reported in al-Jawāhir, makes divorce by bang effective as a deterrent. A further nuance arises when the drinking was coerced or out of necessity: here, al-ʿAllāmah Qāsim in al-Taṣḥīḥ argues that just as the ḥadd punishment is dropped, so too the divorce should not be effective, creating an intra-Hanafi dispute.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
The matn of al-Quduri declares that the divorce of a coerced person (mukrah) and an intoxicated person (sakrān) is effective (wāqiʿ). This is the default position.
Coercion does not invalidate talaq, unlike some contracts.
2
Define 'intoxicated' in the commentary
Al-Yanābīʿ specifies that 'sakrān' refers to intoxication from wine (khamr) or date-wine (nabīdh). Intoxication from bang (narcotic) or medicine does not cause divorce by consensus.
Bang is a plant-based narcotic.
3
Note the later fatwa on bang
Al-Jawāhir reports that in later times, divorce by a person intoxicated with bang is effective as a deterrent (zajran), and this is the muftā bihī.
This changes the earlier consensus.
4
Identify the sub-scenario: coercion/necessity to drink wine
The text then addresses a specific case: what if a person was coerced into drinking wine, or drank it out of necessity, became intoxicated, and divorced?
This is separate from the general rule.
5
Present the two Hanafi positions
Al-Zāhidī holds that the divorce is effective regardless of how the wine was consumed. Al-ʿAllāmah Qāsim disagrees: he argues that just as the ḥadd punishment is dropped in such circumstances, the divorce should also not occur. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilāf.
The text does not give a definitive tarjīḥ.
6
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilāf is that in a case of coerced drinking, some judges may rule the divorce ineffective, while others uphold it. For bang, the later fatwa settles the issue.
This affects marital status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطلاق المكره والسكران واقع. قال في الينابيع: يريد بالسكران الذي سكر من الخمر، أو من النبيذ، أما إذا سكر من البنج، أو من الدواء: لا يقع طلاقه بالإجماع. قال في الجواهر: وفي هذا الزمان إذا سكر بالبنج: يقع طلاقه؛ زجراً له، وعليه الفتوى. ثم الطلاق بالسكر من الخمر واقع، سواء شربها طوعاً، أو كرهاً، أو مضطراً، قاله الزاهدي، كذا في التصحيح. لكن قال العلامة قاسم في التصحيح: ولو أكره على شرب الخمر، أو شرب الخمر للضرورة، وسكر، وطلق: اختلفوا فيه والصحيح أنه كما لا يلزمه الحد، لا يقع طلاقه. اهـ، وكذلك في ابن عابدين 130/9.
And the divorce of a coerced person and an intoxicated person is effective. It is said in al-Yanabi': He means by 'intoxicated' one who is drunk from wine or from nabidh; but if he is intoxicated from bang or from medicine, his divorce does not occur by consensus. It is said in al-Jawahir: And in this time, if one is intoxicated by bang, his divorce occurs, as a deterrent, and upon it is the fatwa. Then divorce due to intoxication from wine is effective, whether he drank it willingly, or unwillingly, or under duress, said by al-Zahidi, likewise in al-Tashih. But al-Allamah Qasim said in al-Tashih: And if one is coerced into drinking wine, or drinks wine out of necessity, gets drunk, and divorces: there is disagreement on it, and the correct view is that just as hadd is not imposed on him, his divorce does not occur. End quote, and likewise in Ibn Abidin 9/130.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage deals with the validity of divorce uttered by a coerced person and an intoxicated person. The matn of al-Quduri states that both are effective. The sharh then clarifies the definition of 'intoxicated': it refers to one who is drunk from wine or date-wine (nabidh). If the intoxication is from bang (a narcotic) or medicine, there is a consensus that the divorce does not occur. However, al-Jawahir reports that in this era (the later period), divorce by one intoxicated with bang is considered effective as a deterrent, and this is the position relied upon for fatwa. The text then discusses divorce from wine intoxication: it is effective regardless of whether the person drank willingly, under coercion, or out of necessity. This is the view of al-Zahidi and is found in al-Tashih. However, there is a conflicting view: al-Allamah Qasim in al-Tashih states that if a person was coerced into drinking wine, or drank out of necessity, and then became intoxicated and divorced, the correct position is that just as the hadd punishment is not applied to him, his divorce is also not effective. This indicates an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on this specific scenario. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) seems to be the first one from al-Zahidi, but the text includes both positions.

Key Terms
مكره (mukrah): A person coerced into an action; here, one coerced to drink wine.
سكران (sakrān): An intoxicated person; the text distinguishes between intoxication from wine/nabidh and from bang/medicine.
بنج (banj): A narcotic plant that causes intoxication and stupor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Allamah Qasim, why is divorce not effective when a person is coerced to drink wine?
A) Because coercion invalidates all contracts.
B) By analogy to the dropping of hadd punishment.
C) Because the person is not considered intoxicated.
D) Because wine is not a valid substance for intoxication rulings.

Key Takeaway
Divorce by a coerced or intoxicated person is generally effective, but Hanafi scholars differed when the drinking itself was coerced or out of necessity, with al-Allamah Qasim arguing for ineffectiveness by analogy to the dropping of hadd.

Divorce by a Mute Person Through Sign Language
A ruling stating a mute's divorce via sign is valid, with condition that sign is customary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a mute person's divorce by sign is valid and identify the condition that the sign must be customary.

Core Idea
A mute's divorce is valid through customary signs because signs substitute speech out of necessity.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario: a person who cannot speak (mute) wishes to divorce his wife. The Hanafi school rules that if the mute uses a sign that is understood by those who communicate with him, the divorce is effective. The rationale is necessity: since the mute cannot utter the divorce formula, his sign takes its place. We will examine the condition that the sign must be 'accustomed' (muttahada) and understand the underlying principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
The ruling is about a person who is mute (akhras) — unable to speak.
The mute may be born mute or have lost speech.
2
Identify the act
The act is talaq (divorce) initiated by the mute.
3
Identify the means of expression
The mute uses isharah (sign language) instead of spoken words.
Signs include gestures that are conventionally understood.
4
Apply the condition
The sign must be 'al-mahduda lahu' — customary and understood by those who deal with him.
If the sign is ambiguous, the divorce may not be valid.
5
Understand the rationale
The text says the sign 'stands in the place of his speech' due to necessity (darurah).
This is based on the principle that necessity makes permissible what is otherwise not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقع طلاق الأخرس بالإشارة المعهودة له؛ لأنها قائمة مقام عبارته؛ دفعاً للحاجة.
And the divorce of a mute occurs through his accustomed sign; because it stands in the place of his speech, for the necessity of need.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This is a clear ruling: a mute person's divorce is valid when done through his understood sign language. The reason is that signs function as a substitute for spoken words, since a mute cannot speak. This is based on the principle of necessity (darurah). The sign must be 'al-mahduda lahu' (customary and understood by those who communicate with him). There is no dispute among the Hanafis on this.

Key Terms
أخرس (akhras): A person unable to speak; a mute.
إشارة (ishārah): Sign language; understood gestures.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a mute person's divorce by sign valid?
A) Because it is a form of writing
B) Because signs stand in place of speech out of necessity
C) Because all divorces must be by sign
D) Because the mute is not responsible for divorce

Key Takeaway
A mute person's divorce is valid through customary signs, as signs replace speech out of necessity.

Suspending Divorce on Future Marriage
Ruling on conditional divorce tied to marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of a conditional divorce tied to future marriage and identify the financial and penal consequences under Hanafi law.

Core Idea
A conditional divorce takes effect at the moment of marriage, entitling the wife to half or full dower and blocking hadd due to shubha.

This passage addresses a specific type of suspended divorce (ta'liq) where a man conditions divorce on a future marriage. The Hanafi school holds that such a condition is valid and the divorce occurs instantly upon marriage. The ruling then details the wife's entitlement to dower and the absence of criminal liability due to the presence of legal doubt (shubhah). Let us examine how the text structures this ruling and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional act
Look for the phrase 'إذا أضاف الطلاق إلى النكاح' — the man attaches divorce to marriage. The examples show clear conditional language: 'إن تزوجتك فأنت طالق' or 'كل امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق'. Recognize that this is a suspended divorce (ta'liq) on a future event.
The condition is the marriage itself, not a separate act.
2
Determine the effect of the condition
The text states 'وقع الطلاق عقيب النكاح' — the divorce occurs immediately after marriage. Understand that the divorce takes effect at the very moment the marriage contract is concluded, not before and not with any delay.
The word 'عقيب' indicates immediate succession.
3
Analyze the financial consequences
If the marriage is not consummated, the wife receives half of the specified dower (نصف المهر). If consummation occurs, she receives the full proper dower (مهر المثل). Note that the dower is due because the marriage was valid, even though it ended instantly by divorce.
The divorce does not invalidate the marriage retroactively; the marriage existed momentarily.
4
Examine the penal consequence
The text says 'ولا يجب الحد، لوجود الشبهة' — the hadd punishment for zina is not imposed due to the presence of shubha (legal doubt). The doubt arises from the existence of a valid marriage contract, even though divorce followed immediately. Hanafi principle: shubha prevents hadd.
Shubha here is the valid contract, which creates doubt whether intercourse was lawful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا أضاف الطلاق إلى النكاح: وقع الطلاق عقيب النكاح، وذلك مثل أن يقول لأجنبية: إن تزوجتك فأنت طالق، أو يقول: كل امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق، فإذا تزوجها: طلقت، ووجب لها نصف المهر، فإن دخل بها وجب لها مهر مثلها، ولا يجب الحد، لوجود الشبهة.
And if he attaches divorce to marriage: the divorce occurs immediately after marriage. For example, if he says to a non-related woman: 'If I marry you, you are divorced,' or he says: 'Every woman I marry is divorced,' then when he marries her, she is divorced, and half the dower is obligatory for her. If he has consummated the marriage, then the proper dower (mahr al-mithl) is obligatory for her, and the hadd punishment is not imposed because of the presence of doubt (shubha).
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage explains the consequence of suspending divorce on a future marriage. The condition is immediate upon marriage: the moment the marriage contract is concluded, the wife is divorced. Consequently, she is entitled to half the dower (if divorce occurred before consummation) or the full proper dower if consummated. The hadd punishment for zina does not apply because the marriage, though resulting in immediate divorce, was valid enough to avoid the charge of illicit relations. This is an established Hanafi ruling.

Key Terms
إضافة (idāfah): Legal attachment; suspending a condition on a future event.
عقيب (uqayb): Immediately after; without delay.
شبهة (shubhah): Legal doubt that prevents application of hadd punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to a non-related woman: 'If I marry you, you are divorced.' He marries her and consummates the marriage. What is the wife entitled to?
A) No dower because the marriage ended immediately.
B) Half of the specified dower.
C) Full proper dower (mahr al-mithl).
D) Only the deferred part of the dower.

Key Takeaway
A conditional divorce tied to future marriage takes effect immediately upon marriage, with the wife entitled to half or full dower, and no hadd punishment due to shubha.

Divorce by a Mute Person through Gesture in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling on validity of divorce by a mute person using known gesture

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorce by a mute person and explain the proof based on necessity.

Core Idea
A mute's known gesture stands in place of speech for divorce, by necessity.

In this lesson, we examine a specific case of divorce (talaq) where the spouse is mute. Can a divorce be effected without spoken words? The Hanafi school addresses this with a clear ruling based on rational necessity. We will explore the ruling, its proof, and how it fits within the broader framework of substitute expressions in marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? What is the hukm (sahih)?
2
Locate the proof
What proof does the author cite for the ruling? (دفعاً للحاجة - to repel necessity)
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on necessity.
3
Understand the condition
What condition must the gesture satisfy? (معهودة له: known to the mute person)
If the gesture is not known, it does not count.
4
Consider cross-madhhab difference
Does any other school disagree? Briefly, Shafi'i requires verbal utterance.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقع طلاق الأخرس بالإشارة. [طلاق الأخرس:] (ويقع طلاق الأخرس بالإشارة المعهودة له؛ لأنها قائمة مقام عبارته؛ دفعاً للحاجة.)
And the divorce of a mute person occurs by gesture. [Divorce of a mute:] (The divorce of a mute occurs by the gesture known to him, because it stands in the place of his speech; to repel necessity.)
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that a mute person can validly divorce by using a known gesture. The proof is necessity (دفعاً للحاجة): since a mute cannot speak, gesture serves as a substitute for verbal expression. The gesture must be understood (معهودة له) by convention. This is a branch of the general principle that actions of a mute are considered like expressions when speech is impossible. The passage type is a ruling with rational proof.

Key Terms
الأخرس (al-akhras): A person who is unable to speak (mute).
الإشارة المعهودة (al-isharat al-ma'hooda): The gesture that is conventionally understood by the mute person, such as nodding or hand signs.

Adding Divorce to a Future Marriage (Idafat al-Talaq ila al-Nikah)
Rulings on conditional divorce when attached to future marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a man adds divorce to a future marriage and identify the consequences (mahr, shubha, repetition).

Core Idea
A conditional divorce attached to a future marriage falls immediately upon marriage, with half mahr if not consummated, and no hadd due to shubha.

This lesson covers the first type of conditional divorce: when a man says to an unrelated woman 'If I marry you, you are divorced' or 'Every woman I marry is divorced.' The text explains when the divorce takes effect, the financial consequences, and why the hadd penalty is not applied. It also distinguishes between the words 'in' and 'kull' regarding repetition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act? 'Adding divorce to marriage' (idafat al-talaq ila al-nikah). Ruling: The divorce falls immediately upon marriage (waqa'a aqiba al-nikah).
2
Understand the consequences upon occurrence
When the divorce occurs after marriage, what are the consequences? Half mahr if not consummated; full mahr mithl if consummated; no hadd due to shubha.
3
Examine the linguistic nuance
The text distinguishes between using 'in' and 'kull'. 'In' does not cause repetition if the same woman is remarried; 'kull' applies to each individual woman.
4
Note the condition for validity
The speaker must be the owner of the right of divorce or add it to his ownership (covered in the second part but applicable here).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا أضاف الطلاق إلى النكاح : وَقَعَ عَقِيبَ النكاح، مثل أن يقول : إن تزوجتك فأنتِ طالق، أو يقول : كل امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق. [إضافة الطلاق إلى النكاح :] (وإذا أضاف الطلاق إلى النكاح: وقع الطلاق (عَقِيب النكاح)، وذلك (مثلُ أن يقول لأجنبية : (إن تزوجتك فأنت طالق، أو يقول : كل امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق)، فإذا تزوجها: طلقت، ووجب لها نصف المهر، فإن دخل بها وَجَبَ لها مهر مثلها، ولا يجب الحد، لوجود الشبهة. ثم إذا تزوجها) : لا تطلق ثانياً؛ لأن: «إن»: لا توجب التكرار وأما : «كل»: فإنها توجب تكرار الأفراد، دون الأفعال، حتى لو تزوج امرأةً أخرى: تطلق. وإذا أضاف الطلاق إلى شَرْطٍ : وَقَعَ عَقِيبَ الشرط، مثل أن يقــول لامرأته : إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق. ولا يصح إضافة الطلاق إلا أن يكون الحالف مالكاً، أو يُضيفه إلى ملكه . [إضافة الطلاق إلى شرط :] (وإذا أضاف الطلاق إلى وجود شرط: وَقَعَ عَقِيب) وجود (الشرط)، وذلك (مثلُ أن يقول لامرأته: إن دخلت الدار، فأنتِ طالق)، وهذا بالاتفاق ؛ لأن الملك قائم فى الحال، والظاهر بقاؤه إلى وقت الشرط، ويصير عند وجود الشرط كالمتكلم بالطلاق في ذلك الوقت. (ولا يصح إضافة الطلاق) أي تعليقه (إلا أن يكون الحالف مالكاً للطلاق حين الحلف، كقوله لمنكوحته: إن دخلت الدار، فأنت طالق، أو يضيفه إلى ملكه ، كقوله لأجنبية: إن نكحتك، فأنت طالق.
And if he adds divorce to marriage: it falls immediately after marriage, such as saying: 'If I marry you, you are divorced,' or saying: 'Every woman I marry is divorced.' [Adding divorce to marriage:] (If he adds divorce to marriage: it falls immediately after marriage, such as saying to an unrelated woman: 'If I marry you, you are divorced,' or 'Every woman I marry is divorced.' So when he marries her, she is divorced, and she is entitled to half the mahr; if he consummates, she is entitled to a mahr mithl; and the hadd is not imposed due to the presence of shubha. Then if he marries her [again], she is not divorced a second time because 'in' does not require repetition; as for 'kull,' it requires repetition of individuals not actions, so if he marries another woman, she is divorced.) And if he adds divorce to a condition: it falls immediately after the condition, such as telling his wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced.' And adding divorce is not valid unless the oath-taker is an owner [of the right of divorce] or adds it to his ownership. [Adding divorce to a condition:] (And if he adds divorce to the existence of a condition: it falls immediately after the existence of the condition, such as telling his wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced.' This is by consensus because ownership exists at present and apparently continues until the condition; and at the occurrence of the condition, he becomes like the one pronouncing divorce at that time. And adding divorce, i.e., suspending it, is not valid unless the oath-taker is an owner of the right of divorce at the time of the oath, like saying to his wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' or he adds it to his ownership, like saying to an unrelated woman: 'If I marry you, you are divorced.')
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage covers two types of conditional divorce: (1) adding divorce to marriage (future marriage) and (2) adding divorce to a condition in an existing marriage. In the first type, the divorce falls immediately upon marriage. The commentary explains the consequences: mahr, no hadd due to shubha (doubt). It also clarifies the difference between using 'in' (which does not cause repetition if the same woman is remarried) and 'kull' (which applies to each individual woman). In the second type, if a husband says to his current wife 'if you enter the house, you are divorced,' the divorce occurs upon entry. The condition for validity is that the person uttering the conditional divorce must either be the owner of the right of divorce at that time (i.e., already married) or add it to his ownership (i.e., to a future marriage). This is agreed upon ('bil-ittifaq') because ownership is present and presumed to continue. The passage type is a ruling with rational and consensus-based proof.

Key Terms
إضافة الطلاق (idafat al-talaq): Linking divorce to a future event, such that the divorce occurs when the event happens.
تعليق الطلاق (ta'liq al-talaq): Suspending divorce on a condition; synonymous with idafat al-talaq.
مَالك الطلاق (malik al-talaq): A person who has the right to divorce, i.e., the husband in an existing marriage.
شبهة (shubha): Doubt regarding the legality of an act, which prevents the application of hadd punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man says to an unrelated woman 'If I marry you, you are divorced' and then marries her, what is the ruling regarding the mahr?
A) She is entitled to the full mahr.
B) She is entitled to half the mahr if the marriage is not consummated.
C) She is entitled to nothing.
D) She is entitled to the mahr but must return it because the divorce invalidates the marriage.

Key Takeaway
When divorce is added to a future marriage, the divorce takes effect upon marriage, with half mahr due and no hadd due to shubha.

Adding Divorce to a Condition in an Existing Marriage (Idafat al-Talaq ila Shart)
Rulings on conditional divorce when attached to a condition in existing marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a husband adds divorce to a condition in an existing marriage and identify the condition for validity (ownership of divorce right).

Core Idea
A conditional divorce in an existing marriage falls upon occurrence of the condition, and the utterance is valid only if the speaker owns the right of divorce at that time.

This lesson covers the second type: a husband telling his current wife 'If you enter the house, you are divorced.' The text explains that the divorce falls upon the fulfillment of the condition, and it is valid by consensus because the husband already owns the right of divorce. It also emphasizes that for any conditional divorce, the speaker must either be the owner of the right of divorce or add it to his ownership (as in the future marriage case).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Adding divorce to a condition in an existing marriage. Ruling: The divorce falls immediately after the condition (waqa'a aqiba al-shart).
2
Understand the basis for validity
It is valid by consensus (bil-ittifaq) because the husband currently owns the right of divorce, and ownership is presumed to continue.
3
Recognize the general condition
The text states a universal condition: 'adding divorce is not valid unless the oath-taker is an owner or adds it to his ownership.' This applies to both types.
4
Note the implication
Only the husband (or someone who owns the right of divorce) can validly pronounce a conditional divorce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا أضاف الطلاق إلى النكاح : وَقَعَ عَقِيبَ النكاح، مثل أن يقول : إن تزوجتك فأنتِ طالق، أو يقول : كل امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق. [إضافة الطلاق إلى النكاح :] (وإذا أضاف الطلاق إلى النكاح: وقع الطلاق (عَقِيب النكاح)، وذلك (مثلُ أن يقول لأجنبية : (إن تزوجتك فأنت طالق، أو يقول : كل امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق)، فإذا تزوجها: طلقت، ووجب لها نصف المهر، فإن دخل بها وَجَبَ لها مهر مثلها، ولا يجب الحد، لوجود الشبهة. ثم إذا تزوجها) : لا تطلق ثانياً؛ لأن: «إن»: لا توجب التكرار وأما : «كل»: فإنها توجب تكرار الأفراد، دون الأفعال، حتى لو تزوج امرأةً أخرى: تطلق. وإذا أضاف الطلاق إلى شَرْطٍ : وَقَعَ عَقِيبَ الشرط، مثل أن يقــول لامرأته : إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق. ولا يصح إضافة الطلاق إلا أن يكون الحالف مالكاً، أو يُضيفه إلى ملكه . [إضافة الطلاق إلى شرط :] (وإذا أضاف الطلاق إلى وجود شرط: وَقَعَ عَقِيب) وجود (الشرط)، وذلك (مثلُ أن يقول لامرأته: إن دخلت الدار، فأنتِ طالق)، وهذا بالاتفاق ؛ لأن الملك قائم فى الحال، والظاهر بقاؤه إلى وقت الشرط، ويصير عند وجود الشرط كالمتكلم بالطلاق في ذلك الوقت. (ولا يصح إضافة الطلاق) أي تعليقه (إلا أن يكون الحالف مالكاً للطلاق حين الحلف، كقوله لمنكوحته: إن دخلت الدار، فأنت طالق، أو يضيفه إلى ملكه ، كقوله لأجنبية: إن نكحتك، فأنت طالق.
And if he adds divorce to marriage: it falls immediately after marriage, such as saying: 'If I marry you, you are divorced,' or saying: 'Every woman I marry is divorced.' [Adding divorce to marriage:] (If he adds divorce to marriage: it falls immediately after marriage, such as saying to an unrelated woman: 'If I marry you, you are divorced,' or 'Every woman I marry is divorced.' So when he marries her, she is divorced, and she is entitled to half the mahr; if he consummates, she is entitled to a mahr mithl; and the hadd is not imposed due to the presence of shubha. Then if he marries her [again], she is not divorced a second time because 'in' does not require repetition; as for 'kull,' it requires repetition of individuals not actions, so if he marries another woman, she is divorced.) And if he adds divorce to a condition: it falls immediately after the condition, such as telling his wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced.' And adding divorce is not valid unless the oath-taker is an owner [of the right of divorce] or adds it to his ownership. [Adding divorce to a condition:] (And if he adds divorce to the existence of a condition: it falls immediately after the existence of the condition, such as telling his wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced.' This is by consensus because ownership exists at present and apparently continues until the condition; and at the occurrence of the condition, he becomes like the one pronouncing divorce at that time. And adding divorce, i.e., suspending it, is not valid unless the oath-taker is an owner of the right of divorce at the time of the oath, like saying to his wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' or he adds it to his ownership, like saying to an unrelated woman: 'If I marry you, you are divorced.')
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage covers two types of conditional divorce: (1) adding divorce to marriage (future marriage) and (2) adding divorce to a condition in an existing marriage. In the first type, the divorce falls immediately upon marriage. The commentary explains the consequences: mahr, no hadd due to shubha (doubt). It also clarifies the difference between using 'in' (which does not cause repetition if the same woman is remarried) and 'kull' (which applies to each individual woman). In the second type, if a husband says to his current wife 'if you enter the house, you are divorced,' the divorce occurs upon entry. The condition for validity is that the person uttering the conditional divorce must either be the owner of the right of divorce at that time (i.e., already married) or add it to his ownership (i.e., to a future marriage). This is agreed upon ('bil-ittifaq') because ownership is present and presumed to continue. The passage type is a ruling with rational and consensus-based proof.

Key Terms
إضافة الطلاق (idafat al-talaq): Linking divorce to a future event, such that the divorce occurs when the event happens.
تعليق الطلاق (ta'liq al-talaq): Suspending divorce on a condition; synonymous with idafat al-talaq.
مَالك الطلاق (malik al-talaq): A person who has the right to divorce, i.e., the husband in an existing marriage.
شبهة (shubha): Doubt regarding the legality of an act, which prevents the application of hadd punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his wife, 'If you cook dinner tonight, you are divorced.' She cooks dinner. According to the Hanafi school, what happens?
A) The divorce does not occur because cooking is not a valid condition.
B) The divorce occurs immediately upon her cooking dinner.
C) The divorce occurs only if the husband repeats the phrase after dinner.
D) The divorce is invalid because the condition is trivial.

Key Takeaway
When divorce is added to a condition in an existing marriage, the divorce occurs upon fulfillment of the condition, provided the husband owns the right of divorce at the time of the statement.
