Conditional Divorce: Ownership Requirement and Particles
Ruling with proof and explanation of conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for a valid conditional divorce in the Hanafi school and apply the ruling to distinguish between the particles 'in' and 'kull' in determining whether divorce occurs once or repeatedly.

Core Idea
A conditional divorce is valid only if the husband owns the right to divorce at the time of the oath or attaches it to future ownership.

The ability to suspend divorce upon a future condition is a recognized mechanism in Hanafi law. However, its validity hinges on the husband's proprietary power over divorce at the moment of the oath. This lesson unpacks the two scenarios where conditional divorce is effective and then examines how different conditional particles affect the scope of divorce—whether it occurs once or is repeated with each subject.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling on conditional divorce
Read the opening sentences: divorce occurs immediately upon the condition (e.g., 'If you enter the house, you are divorced'). This is valid by consensus (bil ittifaq).
Consensus (ijma) is the proof here.
2
Extract the ownership requirement for validity
The text states that the oath-taker must be an owner of the right to divorce at the time of the oath, or he must attach the condition to his future ownership (e.g., saying to a non-wife: 'If I marry you…'). If neither condition is met, the conditional divorce is void.
This is a condition (shart) for validity.
3
Analyze the particle 'in' vs 'kull'
The particle 'in' (if) does not imply repetition; divorce occurs once when the condition is fulfilled. 'Kull' (every) implies repetition of individuals—so if a man says 'Every woman I marry is divorced', each new marriage triggers a separate divorce.
'Kull' is not a true conditional particle but is analogous because it ties the result to a noun.
4
Apply the rules to given scenarios
Work through the examples: (1) a man says to his wife 'If you enter the house, you are divorced' – divorce occurs once upon entry. (2) a man says to a non-wife 'If you enter the house, you are divorced' then marries her – no divorce because he was not owner and did not attach to future ownership. (3) a man says 'Every woman I marry is divorced' – each marriage results in divorce.
The same principle applies to all conditional particles listed: in, idha, idha ma, kull, kullama, mata, mata ma.

(و: كلما ومتى، ومتى ما، ونحو ذلك، كـ: لو، نحو: أنت كذا لو دخلت الدار. * (ففي كل هذه الشروط إذا وجد الشرط في ملكه: انحلت اليمين، ووقع الطلاق؛ لأنها غير مقتضية للعموم والتكرار، فبوجود)
And when he attaches divorce to a condition: it occurs immediately upon the condition, such as when he says to his wife: If you enter the house, you are divorced. And attaching divorce is not valid unless the oath-taker is an owner, or he attaches it to his ownership. Then if he marries her: she is not divorced a second time; because 'in' does not necessitate repetition, but 'kull' necessitates repetition of individuals, not actions, so if he marries another woman: she becomes divorced. Attaching divorce to a condition: (And when he attaches divorce to the existence of a condition: it occurs immediately upon the existence of the condition, such as his saying to his wife: If you enter the house, you are divorced, and this is by agreement; because ownership exists at present, and the apparent is its continuation until the time of the condition, and he becomes at the existence of the condition like the one speaking divorce at that time. (And attaching divorce is not valid) i.e., suspending it (unless the oath-taker is an owner of divorce at the time of the oath, like his saying to his wife: If you enter the house, you are divorced, or he attaches it to his ownership, like his saying to a non-wife: If I marry you, you are divorced. And the particles of condition are: 'in', and 'idha', and 'idha ma', and 'kull', and 'kullama', and 'mata', and 'mata ma'. So in all these conditions, when the condition occurs in his ownership: the oath is dissolved, and divorce occurs. (And if he was not an owner of divorce at the time of the oath, and did not attach it to ownership, by saying to a non-wife: If you enter the house, you are divorced, then marries her, and she enters the house: she does not become divorced; due to the lack of ownership at the time of the oath, and attachment to it, and one of them is necessary. [Particles of condition:] (And the particles of condition: 'in' with kasra on the hamzah, and 'idha', and 'idha ma', and 'kull', and this is not a condition in reality, because what follows it is a noun, and the condition is what the result is attached to, and results are attached to actions, but it is annexed to the condition; due to the attachment of the action to the noun that follows it, as you say: every woman I marry, then such. Durar. (And 'kullama' and 'mata', and 'mata ma', and the like, such as 'law', like: You are such if you enter the house. * (So in all these conditions, when the condition occurs in his ownership: the oath is dissolved, and divorce occurs; because they do not necessitate generality and repetition, so with existence)
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the matn of Al-Quduri regarding the topic of conditional divorce. The author explains that when a husband suspends divorce upon a condition, the divorce takes effect immediately upon the fulfillment of that condition. This is by consensus (بالاتفاق) because the husband's ownership of the right to divorce is present at the time of the oath and is presumed to continue until the condition occurs. The validity of such a conditional divorce requires that the husband either be the owner of the right to divorce at the time of the oath (i.e., the marriage must exist) or that he attach the condition to his future ownership (e.g., saying to a non-wife: 'If I marry you, you are divorced'). If he says to a non-wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' and later marries her, the divorce does not occur upon her entering the house because at the time of the oath he was not the owner and did not attach it to his ownership. The text then lists the particles used for conditions: 'in' (if), 'idha' and 'idha ma' (when), 'kull' (every), 'kullama' (whenever), 'mata' and 'mata ma' (when). For all these, if the condition occurs while the wife is in his marriage, the oath is dissolved and divorce takes place. The particle 'in' does not imply repetition, whereas 'kull' implies repetition of individuals (e.g., 'every woman I marry...') but not actions. Thus, if a man says 'If you enter the house, you are divorced' to his wife, it occurs once. If he says 'Every woman I marry is divorced' and marries multiple women, each marriage results in divorce.

Key Terms
إضافة الطلاق إلى شرط (idafat al-talaq ila shart): The act of suspending the occurrence of divorce upon the fulfillment of a future condition, e.g., 'If you enter the house, you are divorced.'
إن (in): A conditional particle meaning 'if' that does not imply repetition; the divorce occurs once upon fulfillment of the condition.
كل (kull): A particle meaning 'every' that implies repetition of individuals; when used in conditional divorce, it applies to each subject individually.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to his wife: 'If you go to your parents' home, you are divorced.' She goes once. Later, she goes again. How many divorces occur according to the Hanafi school?
A) Two divorces, because each action is a separate condition.
B) One divorce, because 'in' does not imply repetition.
C) No divorce, because the condition is not clear.
D) Three divorces, because the marriage ends.

Key Takeaway
A conditional divorce is valid only if the husband owns the right to divorce at the time of the oath or explicitly attaches the condition to future ownership; the particle 'in' triggers once, while 'kull' triggers for each individual subject.

Conditional Divorce Before Marriage: The Hanafi Rule
Ruling on conditional divorce before marriage with reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi condition for validity of conditional divorce (ownership at time of oath or attribution to future ownership) to determine whether a conditional divorce is effective.

Core Idea
For conditional divorce to be effective, ownership of talaq must exist at the time of the condition or be linked to a future ownership.

This passage from al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Quduri addresses a common scenario: a man pronounces a conditional divorce to a woman who is not his wife. The Hanafi school treats conditional divorces as oaths, and the ruling hinges on the speaker's ownership of the right to divorce at the time of the condition. Understanding this principle prevents common misunderstandings about suspended divorces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
Recognize that the man said 'If you enter the house, you are divorced' to a non-related woman (ajnabiyyah). At that moment, she was not his wife.
The woman is ajnabiyyah, meaning marriage with her is permissible and no existing marriage bond.
2
State the ruling
After marrying her, she enters the house: the divorce does not take effect. She remains married.
This is the explicit ruling from the matn.
3
Extract the legal condition for effectiveness
The commentary explains that for a conditional divorce to be effective, either the speaker must own the right to divorce at the time of the oath (i.e., she is his wife) OR the condition must be explicitly attributed to a future time when he will own it (e.g., 'if I marry you').
This is the key principle (qā‘idah) governing ta‘līq al-ṭalāq.
4
Apply the condition to the scenario
Here, neither condition is met: at the time of the oath, he did not own the right to divorce her, nor did he attribute the condition to a future marriage.
Therefore, the conditional divorce is null and void.
5
Contrast with a valid scenario
If he had said 'If I marry you, you are divorced,' then married her, and she entered the house, the divorce would occur because he attributed the condition to the moment of ownership (marriage).
This contrast solidifies the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قال لأجنبية : إن دخلت الدار فأنتِ طالق، ثم تزوجها، فدخلت الدار : لم تَطْلُق. (وإن لم يكن مالكاً للطلاق حين الحلف، ولم يُضفه إلى ملك، بأن قال لأجنبية: إن دخلت الدار، فأنتِ طالق، ثم تزوجها، فدخلت الدار : لم تَطلُق ؛ لعدم الملك حين الحلف، والإضافة إليه، ولا بد من واحد منهما.)
If he said to a non-related woman: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' then he married her, and she entered the house: she is not divorced. (If he was not the owner of divorce at the time of the oath, and he did not attribute it to ownership - such as if he said to a non-related woman: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' then he married her, and she entered the house: she is not divorced due to the lack of ownership at the time of the oath and attribution to it; one of the two is necessary.)
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents a specific ruling from the Hanafi school regarding conditional divorce. The matn states the ruling clearly: if a man says to a woman who is a non-mahram (ajnabiyyah) 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' then later marries her, and she enters the house, the divorce does not take effect. The commentary explains the reasoning: the divorce is ineffective because at the time of the oath (hulf), the man did not have the right to divorce her (since she was not his wife), and he also did not link the condition to a future time when he would own the right (by attaching it to marriage). For a conditional divorce to be effective, one of two conditions must be met: either the speaker must have the right to divorce at the time of the oath (meaning she is his wife), or the condition must be explicitly linked to a future act of ownership (e.g., 'if I marry you'). Here, neither is present, so the condition fails and no divorce occurs.

Key Terms
أجنبية (ajnabiyyah): A woman who is not a mahram and not currently married to the speaker; a non-related woman.
يمين (yamin): An oath, which in this context is the conditional pronouncement of divorce treated legally as a vow or oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) She is divorced only if he intended divorce at the time of marriage.
D) The divorce is suspended until the condition is repeated after marriage.

Key Takeaway
For a conditional divorce to be effective, the speaker must either own the right to divorce at the time of the condition or explicitly link the condition to a future time when he will own that right.

Condition Words in Divorce: General Rule and the Exception of 'Kullama'
Explains condition words in divorce and their legal effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for condition words in divorce, distinguishing between words that trigger a single divorce and 'kullama' which triggers repetitive divorces up to three, and evaluate the effect of removal of ownership on the oath.

Core Idea
Most condition words trigger divorce once; 'kullama' repeats up to three; removal of ownership by three divorces invalidates the oath.

This lesson dives into a key passage from the commentary on Al-Quduri's Al-Mukhtasar, where the author, Al-Maydani, explains the legal effects of various condition words used in divorce oaths. Understanding the precise operation of these words is crucial for a mufti or judge when a husband utters a conditional divorce. The passage distinguishes between ordinary condition words that are satisfied once, and 'kullama' which, due to its implication of generality, leads to repeated divorces. It also addresses what happens when the husband loses his right to divorce (ownership) after the oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition words
Recognize the list: in, idha, idha ma, kullu, kullama, mata, mata ma, law. Note that 'kullu' is not a true condition but annexed due to similarity.
2
Apply the general rule for most condition words
When the condition occurs while the wife is still in marriage, the condition is fulfilled and one divorce occurs. The oath then ends because these words do not imply generality or repetition.
3
Recognize the exception for 'kullama'
This word implies generality and repetition. Each time the condition occurs, a divorce takes effect, continuing until three divorces are exhausted.
After three, the wife becomes haram until she marries another man and is divorced.
4
Determine the effect after three divorces and remarriage
If the couple remarries after a proper intermediate marriage, and the condition repeats, no divorce occurs according to the majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad). Zufar dissents, holding the oath revives.
The ikhtilaf is within the Hanafi school; the mufta bihi is the majority view.
5
Understand the effect of removal of ownership (zawal al-milk) on the oath
If the husband divorces once or twice, the oath remains valid because the subject (mahall) still partially exists. If he divorces three times, the oath is invalidated due to complete removal of the subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وألفاظ الشرط : إن ، و : إذا، و : إذا ما، و: كلُّ، و: كلما، و متی، و : متى ما . إن : بكسر الهمزة و إذا، و إذا ما، و كلُّ، وهذا ليس بشرط حقيقة، لأن ما يليها اسم، والشرط ما يتعلق به الجزاء، والأجزية تتعلق بالأفعال لكنه ألحق بالشرط؛ لتعلق الفعل بالاسم الذي يليها كقولك كل امرأة أتزوجها، فكذا. درر» و: كلما و متی، و : متى ما ، ونحو ذلك، كـ: لو، نحو: أنت كذا لو دخلت الدار. (ففي كل هذه الشروط إذا وُجِدَ الشرط في ملكه: انحلت اليمين، ووقع الطلاق) ؛ لأنها غير مقتضية للعموم والتكرار، فبوجود الفعل مرة يتم الشرط، ولا بقاء لليمين بدونه. إلا في : كلما : فإن الطلاق يتكرَّرُ بتكرار الشرط حتى تقع ثلاث تطليقات . فإن تزوجها بعد ذلك، وتكرر الشرط : لم يقع شيء. وزوال الملك بعد اليمين : لا يبطلها، (إلا في: كلما، فإن الطلاق يتكرر بتكرار الشرط)؛ لأنها تقتضي تعميم الأفعال، ومن ضرورة التعميم: التكرار، (حتى تقع ثلاث تطليقات)، وينتهي الحل بزوال المحلية. (فإن تزوجها بعد ذلك (۱) ، وتكرر الشرط: لم يقع شيء)؛ لأن باستيفاء الطلقات الثلاث المملوكات في هذا النكاح: لم يبق الجزاء، وبقاء اليمين به وبالشرط وفيه خلاف زفر. «هداية» (وزوال الملك) بطلقة، أو اثنتين ( بعد اليمين: لا يبطلها): أي لا يبطل اليمين، لأنه لا يوجد الشرط، فبقي والجزاء باق؛ لبقاء محله، فبقي اليمين. قيَّدنا زوال الملك بالطلقة أو الثنتين؛ لأنه إذا زال بثلاث طلقات : فإنه يبطل اليمين، لزوال المحلية. (1) أي فيما إذا تزوجها بعد زوج آخر ، وتكرر الشرط، فعند زفر يقع الطلاق. البناية ١٨٠/٥ .
The condition words are: 'in' (with kasra on the alif), 'idha', 'idha ma', 'kullu', 'kullama', 'mata', 'mata ma'. 'In' (with kasra on the alif), 'idha', 'idha ma', and 'kullu' - this last is not a real condition because what follows it is a noun, while a condition is that on which the consequence depends, and consequences depend on actions, but it is annexed to conditions due to the attachment of the action to the noun that follows it, as in your saying: 'Every woman I marry, then such-and-such.' (Durar). And 'kullama', 'mata', 'mata ma', and similar, such as 'law', like your saying: 'You are such-and-such if you enter the house.' (In all these conditions, if the condition occurs while he is the owner [of divorce], the oath dissolves and divorce occurs) because they do not imply generality and repetition; so with the occurrence of the action once, the condition is fulfilled and there is no prolongation of the oath without it. Except in 'kullama': for divorce is repeated with repetition of the condition until three divorces occur. Then if he marries her after that (i.e., after another husband), and the condition repeats, nothing occurs. And removal of ownership after the oath does not invalidate it. (Except in 'kullama': for divorce is repeated with repetition of the condition) because it implies generality of actions, and from the necessity of generality is repetition. (Until three divorces occur), and the permissibility ends with the removal of the subject. (Then if he marries her after that (1), and the condition repeats, nothing occurs) because with the exhaustion of the three owned divorces in this marriage, the consequence does not remain, and the oath remains with it and with the condition, and there is a difference of opinion from Zufar. (Hidayah) (And removal of ownership) by one or two divorces (after the oath does not invalidate it): i.e., it does not invalidate the oath, because the condition has not occurred, so it remains and the consequence remains due to the persistence of its locus, so the oath remains. We limited removal of ownership to one or two divorces because if it is removed by three divorces, then it invalidates the oath due to the removal of the subject. (1) i.e., if he marries her after another husband, and the condition repeats, according to Zufar the divorce occurs. (Al-Binayah 5/180).
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition from the commentary on Al-Quduri's matn, covering the legal implications of condition words (alfaz al-shart) in divorce. The text first lists the common condition words: 'in' (if), 'idha' (when), 'idha ma' (whenever), 'kullu' (every), 'kullama' (every time), 'mata' (when), 'mata ma' (whenever). It notes that 'kullu' is not a true condition in the sense of a verb-based condition because it is followed by a noun, but its legal effect is similar to conditions because the implied action relates to the noun. The core ruling is: for all these condition words (except 'kullama'), if the condition occurs while the wife is still in his marriage (i.e., he still has the right to divorce), the sworn condition is fulfilled and divorce occurs once. The reasoning is that these words do not imply generality or repetition; once the action happens, the condition is satisfied and the oath ends. However, 'kullama' is an exception because it implies repetition: each time the condition is met, a divorce occurs until three divorces are exhausted. After three divorces, the wife becomes haram to him until she marries another man and is divorced. If he marries her again after that (after a valid intermediate marriage), and the condition repeats, no further divorce occurs because the divorce consequences of that oath have been exhausted; there is a known disagreement with Zufar, who holds that a new divorce can occur. The passage also addresses the effect of loss of ownership (zawal al-milk) after the oath: if the husband divorces her once or twice, the oath remains valid because the subject of divorce still exists (she is still partially his wife). But if the ownership is completely removed by three divorces, the oath becomes invalid because there is no subject left for the divorce to affect.

Key Terms
كلما (kullama): A condition word meaning 'every time' that implies generality and repetition of the action, thus triggering divorce with each occurrence until three.
زوال الملك (zawal al-milk): Loss of marital ownership/authority over the wife, i.e., divorce that ends the marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to his wife: 'Every time you enter the kitchen, you are divorced.' She enters the kitchen three times. According to the Hanafi school, what is the status of the marriage after the third entry?
A) The marriage continues as normal.
B) She is divorced thrice, making her haram until she marries another man.
C) She is divorced only once, and the condition is exhausted.
D) The divorce is suspended until the condition is repeated in a new marriage.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school limits the repetitive effect of 'kullama' to three divorces, after which the oath is exhausted regardless of subsequent remarriage.
